This report is based upon the findings of an appraisal mission that visited Mexico in June 1991.
Mission members were Robert Kaplan (mission leader); Sergio Margulis (Natural Resource
Economist); John McKenna (Senior Resource Planner); Claudia Alderman (Protected Areas
Specialist); Adrian Demayo (Water Pollution Specialist, consultant); Luiz Antonio Prado
(Pollution Control Systems Specialist, consultant); Javier de la Maza (Conservation Specialist,
consultant); Kenneth Rock (Environmental Assessments Specialist; consultant); Maria Gomez-
Taylor (Environmental Laboratories Specialist, consultant); and Ellen Newfield (Information
Systems Specialist). Emmanuel Jimenez (Principal Economist); Gunnar Eskeland (Economist);
and Joost Draaisma (Economist) also joined the mission for a few days. Robert Kaplan compieted
appraisal of the protected areas program on a mission to Mexico in February 1992.

ENVIROMENTAL PROJECT
LOAN AND PROJECT SUMMARY
Banco Nacional de Obras y Servicios Publicos -- BANOBRAS
Guarantor:       United Mexican States
Ecguilng
Ag_ncy:          Secretariat for Urban Development and Ecology -- SEDUE
Amogunt:         US$50 million equivalent; GET Grant US$30 million equivalent
loan Terms:      Repayment in 15 years, including 5 years of grace, with interest at the
Bank's standard variable rate
Beneficiaries:    SEDUE and State environmental entities
£wiect
Cabiectives:     The overall objective of the proposed project would be to support the
government's ability to carry out key environmental protection and natural
resource management functions in the short term and at the same time to
strengthen the institutional and policy framework to enable the government to
carry out these functions in a more efficient and decentralized manner in the
future. The project would support the government's strategy of transforming
SEDUE's Subsecretaria de Ecologia (SEDIT/SE) over time into a highly
qualified second tier organization that supervises and coordinates
environmental work carried out by other levels of government or other
federal agencies, or under contract to private sector firms.
pescription:     The project would finance the first phase of the government's environmental
program concentrating on key functions of SEDUE/SE and setting the stage
for a more decentralized approach, plus activities and studies to support
preparation of a second phase which would focus on state-level
environmental agencies, building on the experience of the first phase. The
first phase would have three components. Compgnent-I would finance key
environmental services, namely monitoring and controlling water and air
pollution (29%), minimizing potential negative environmental impacts of
public and private sector investment projects (11%) and conserving
biodiversity (44%). Component II would strengthen sector management and
administration, through institutional strengthening of SEDUE/SE (4%), a
pilot decentralization prograrn with up to five states (5%), and the
commissioning of special studies to explore the use of economic instruments
to complement the current heavily command and control approach to



- v -
environmeatal management (1%). Component III would support the
strengthening of the regulatory framework through the preparation of
additional technical standards for pollution control and naturai resource
protection (3%). Preparation for the second phase of the government's
sectoral program would involve carrying out four pre-investment studies and
financing the activities of a cocrdinating unit reporting to the SEDUE's
Under-Secretary for Ecology to prepare the program (3%).
Bfflnerits:       Mexico's environmental problems are grave. The country can reap
significant benefits from investing in establishing an adequate institutional
and policy framework for systematically addressing these problems. The key
functions component would result in reduced air and water pollution,
systematic evaluation of potential negative environmental effects of public
and private sector investments and design of mitigating activities, and
protection of endemic and endangered species, and biological diversity of
global importance.
Risks:            By the time of the closing date (December 31, 1995), the proposed project is
expected to increase the recurrent costs of SEDUE/SE by about 30 percent
above the current level. Given that the SEDUE/SE's 1991 budget accounts
for less than 0.2 percent of the total federal budget, this increase would be
fully within the absorptive capacity of the federal budget if the current strong
political commitment holds. Two potential risks are that: (i) a decline in
apparent strong current political commitment to strengthen SEDUE/SE
would lead to the problems of inaction, failure to provide adequate
counterpart funds in a timely manner, and a slow pace of disbursement; and
(ii) if some cost recovery or other mechanisms for ensuring a sustainable
resource base for SEDUE/SE are not achieved by the close of the proposed
project, SEDUE/SE's ability to sustain increased recurrent costs would be
subject to the same budgetary uncertainty that led to the weakening of the
agency in the past. Project design minimizes these risks by supporting the
creation of an inter-secretarial task force to address the issues of sector
financing and the use of economic instruments, and by including in the
project provisions for monitoring the adequacy of the budget. Regarding the
proposed protected areas program (to be financed by a GET grant), there are
two potential risks: (i) a technical risk that SEDUE would have difficulty in
producing in a timely manner management plans deemed satisfactory to
protect the biodiversity in the targeted protected areas; and (ii) a managerial
risk that SEDUE would have difficulty in managing effectively the protected
areas program. The project would provide technical assistance to SEDUE to
minimize both risks to an acceptable level.

1. INTROUTQ
1.1   In 1982, after three decades of high growth and increasing government involvement in the
economy, Mexico entered a period of economic crisis. Faced by rising Interest rates in
international financial markets and falling oil prices, the country was no longer able to generate
the resources necessary to finance costly public policies and programs that were put in place
during the preceding period of prosperity. In response to the crisis, the government took strong
measures to stabilize the economy and open it to foreign competition, especially during the second
half of the decade. Key reforms during this period included the dismantling of trade barriers,
liberalization of financial markets, tax reform, and privatization of public enterprise. As a result
of these comprehensive policy measures, Mexico has become one of the most open economies in
the world, and growth rates thave again turned significantly positive. At the same time, shrinking
budgets of sectoral secretariats (min:stries) that were necessary to bring the federal budget under
control have led to significant weakening of several of the of these secretariats. The country is
now turning its attention to defining the appropriate role of the public sector in an open economy,
and to strengthening secretariats to carry out important government functions.
1.2    Enivironmental problems in Mexico have been exacerbated by several decades of
industrialization without assessment of, or attention to, environmental costs. A number of policy
failures have contributed to such problems, includir-: (i) weak institutional capacity, which has
resulted in weak enforcement of legislation; (ii) agricultural policies which led to excessive use of
natural resources and increased rural-urban migration; (iii) subsidies for pasture and cattle
ranching, which promoted deforestation and consequent soil erosion; and (iv) subsidies to
consumers of natural resources, which encouraged excessive demand, waste and corresponding
investments in infrastructure for resource supply and waste treatment.
1.3    The Mexican population is heavily urban, with over 70 percent of all Mexicans living in
urban areas. About half of the. labor force lives in the three largest cities (Mexico City,
Guadalajara and Monterrey), and almost a quarter of the country's industrial establishments are
located in these cities. This concentration of population and indLtstry has led to serious problems
of air, water and soil pollution. At the same time, in rural areas, development pressures and
illegal trade in wildlife threaten to destroy Mexico's considerable wealth in biodiversity. In the
past, in an effort to reduce population pressure in urban areas, settlement schemes have opened up
previously untouched tracts of forest. The resulting deforestation and uncontrolled trade in
wildlife have put at risk Mexico's wealth in plant and animal life and many endemic and
endangered species are close to extinction.
1.4    Faced with these natural resources management issues, and consistent with economic
principles of allocation of scarce resources, the government has increasingly taken action to force
individuals and institutions to face the social costs of their private decisions. To do so effectively,
the public sector must have the capability to collect information on environmental damages and to
regulate, as appropriate, through command and control procedures or economic incentives and
instruments, the conditions under which these scarce resources may be exploited.



- 2 -
II. ElNVIRONENTALSETOR
A.    KeynvironDn Issues a-PrQoI nm
2.1    Major environmental problems confronting Mexico are: (i) air pollution in major
metropolitan areas, of which the Mexico City Metropolitan Area (MCMA) is the single most
important case; (ii) water scarcity and water pollution, from discharges of untreated wastewaters
from industry and municipalities, and from improper disposal of hazardous wastes; (iii) soil
erosion; and (iv) deforestation and destruction of natural ecosystems which lead to loss of
blodiversity. This section provides a brief background on these environmental issues.
A. I   Air Pollution
2.2    Air pollution affects most of Mexico's largest cities as a result of emissions mainly by
vehicles and by industry. The MCMA is by far the most polluted city in the country, where
nearly 15 million people live, representing almost 20 percent of Mexico's population. An
estimated 4.5 million tons of pollutants are released into the atmosphere of MCMA each year.
Motor vehicle related pollutants account for 82 percent of total emissions; stationary sources,
which include some 30,000 industrial plants, account for 12 percent of the total by volume; and
natural sources, substantially increased by deforestation and erosion, account for the remaining 6
percent.
2.3    The effects of air pollutant emissions in the MCMA are aggravated by geographical and
meteorological conditions which are not propitious to dispersion. The MCMA is located in the
valley of Mexico which is at an average altitude of 2,200 meters, and is surrounded by a
mountain chain where the average altitude is about 3,200 meters, with some peaks exceeding
5,000 meters. In winter times, thermal inversions occur fiom 20-30 days per month. Formation
of pollutarts is also fostered by the high level of insolation in the valley.
2.4    Critical air pollutants in MCMA include ozone, suspended particulate matter, carbon
monoxide and lead. Peak ozone concentrations are more than four times the accepted national
and international norms - the highest of any city in the world. Peak and annual average
particulate concentrations are more than four times the applicable norms. Concentrations of
carbon monoxide, nitrogen and sulfur dioxides and lead also frequently exceed the national and/or
international norms, although to a lesser degree. The health effects associated to these levels of
pollution are expected to be extremely high, as well as the effects on vegetation, material,
visibility, and acid deposition.
A.2   Water Scarcity and Water Pollution
2.5    Mexico is a country with scarce water resources, which are badly distributed spatially,
temporally throughout the year, and in relation to demographic and industrial location. Only 15
percent of surface waters are above the elevation of 500 meters, where 75 percent of the
population lives and where 80 percent of industrial plants are installed. Irrigation of high
elevation arid and semi-arid land accounts for some 85 percent of water use, and is extremely
inefficient. Some 28 billion m3/year are pumped from groundwater for irrigation, with the result



3 -
that over 80 major aquifers are being overexploited. Out of 316 river basins in the country, only
31 carry 92 percent of the organic pollution, and only 5 (Lerma-Santiago, Panico, Balsas Blanco
and San Juan) account for 60 percent organic pollution.
2.6    Water pollution contributes to - --intraction of waterborne diseases, which are still the
number one cause of infant mortality in iv;.ico. Estimates of industrial waste water range from
80-184 m3lsecond, and for municipal waste water they range from 200-255 ms/second'.
Industries generating most of the pollution are oil, paper, foundries, textile, mining, coffee
processing, sugar and leather. As for municipal waste waters, the SEDUE estimates that there
are 246 treatment plants, with an overall capacity to treat 16 m3/second, of which only 8
m3/second are effectively treated. Water quality reports only cover total BOD (biological oxygen
demand), estimated at some 2.2 million tons/year, and parameters such as temperature, pH,
solids, oils, greases and coliform. There is no comprehensive information available on heavy
metals, pesticides and other tcxic substances. Additionally, non-point sources of pollution have
not been studied, while there has been an increasing consumption of agro-chemicals, including
pesticides that have been banned in most industrialized countries2.
2.7    Municipal and Industrial $olid and Hazardous Waste. Contributing to water pollution, at
least 13,000 tons per day of murnicipal solid waste are dumped on abandoned land or thrown in
nearby waterways, while a number of new landfills are inoperable or improperly managed. At
the same time, an estimated 3 m "lion tons of hazardous waste (carcinogenic, mutagenic and
teratogenic substances that are not biodegradable) and some 15 million tons of other industrial
waste are dumped eacn year outside of designated areas or flushed into municipal sewer systems.
2.8    SEDUE estimates that total municipal solid waste generation in the country amounts to 52
thousand tons/day, with the per capita waste ranging from 0.96 kg/day in the Federal District to
0.54 kg/day in other regions. Even though 75 percent of this total are collected, 65 percent of
the collected waste are still inadequately disposed of. The 13,000 tons/day which a&e not
collected, are dumped on streets or abandoned land, or into nearby waterways. A significant
number of landfills are inoperable or improperly managed, largely because tariffs do not cover the
costs of the service.
A.3   Soil Erosion
2.9    Soil erosion and its associated negative impact on agricultural productivity, water
retention, runoff and aquifer recharge are a major environmental problem in Mexico. Satellite
imagery studies executed by the federal Secretariat of Agriculture and Hydraulic Resources
(SARI) reveal that soil erosion is country-wide problem, increasing at an alarming rate, and is
1 The federal Secretariat of Urban Development and Ecology (SEDUE) estimates that the total
capacity of existing municipal waste water treatment plants covers less than 10 percent of
discharges, and reports that 70 percent of such installations are out of service or have much lower
performances than intended.
2 With regard to pesticides, in particular, the major problems are operators' poisoning and
potential foodstuff contamination. However, information on both is scarce and incomplete.



-4-
mostly Irreversible. The most recent survey (1980) indicated that about 80 percent of Mexico's
land is subject to some degree of erosion; recent aerial photograph studies indicate that areas
classified as moderately eroded have now become severely ended. Sevcre erosion Oosses from
11-15 tons/ha/year) now affect about 42 percent of the Mexican territory (83 million hectares).
2.10   On-farm costs of erosion include reduced crop yields, land removed from crop production
or used in less productive enterprises, and additional production costs (such as fertilizers, to
replace lost nutrients). Non-farm losses to erosion include siltation of waterways, dams and other
collection sites, and destruction of the natural resource base in watersheds. International evidence
on the relative weight of on and off-farm costs are conflicting and inconclusive.
2.11   Soil losses affect the ability of the agricultural sector to maintain present levels of
production. SARH estimates that by the turn of the century production of maize will fall by more
than one million tons (or 12.5 percent) without adequate and timely soil and water conservation
measures. Moreover, over one million hectares of productive forests, crop and livestock areas
would be irretrievably lost over the next 7-10 years.
A.4   Loss of Biological Diversity
2.12   Mexico's impressive natural biological resources make it one of the world's 13 "mega-
diversity" countries that together account for about 60 percent of the planet's species. Mexico
ranks fourth in the world in total number of species, first in reptiles, second in mammals, and
fourth in amphibians. Seven of the eight species of the world's marine turtles come to Mexican
shores to lay their eggs. The degree of endemism (species found in no other country) is also very
high - over half of the country's reptiles, amphibians, and plants; nearly half of the country's
freshwater fishes; and approximately one-third of the country's mammals. Because Mexico forms
part of a land bridge between North and South America, and houses a broad variety of
ecosystems, the country is extremely important to migratory species (especially birds, whales,
turtles, butterflies and bats) who spend as much of half of the year in Mexican territory.
2.13   Despite the country's wealth in biological diversity, and the tangible and intangible
present and future benefits to people of Mexico's valuable ecosystems, these resources are under
increasing development pressure. The greatest threats come from conversion of natural habitats,
especially forests, to agricultural uses and cattle ranching. Only one-third of the country remains
under forest (it was once 60 percent), and only 25 percent of the forest lands are considered
"intact" biologically. Another factor has been the lucrative trade in wildlife. Although wildlife
trade is strictly regulated by Mexican law, Mexico is one of Latin America's largest exporters of
wildlife, as regulations are only weakly enforced.
B.    The Legal and Institutional Framework
B. I   Legislation and Regulations
2.14   Environmental legislation in Mexico is generally adequate. Article 27 of the Mexican
Constitution establishes the obligation of the federal government to conserve the nation's natural



resources and to preserve and restore ecological equilibrium in the public interest. Article 73
gives the Congress the authority to promulga8.e laws defining the respective roles of the federal,
state and local levels of government in environmental protection. Exercising this constitutional
authority, the Congress passed the Ley General del Equilibrio Ecologico y la Proteccion al
Ambiente (General Ecology Law) which went into effect in March 1988. It contains rules which
define ecological planning and zoning, environmental impact studies, restoration of degraded
ecosystems, protection of natural areas and plant and animal life, sound exploitation of natural
resources, and pollution prevention and control. The Law contains the framework for ensuring a
more sensitive and sustainable development pattern in Mexico through improved intersectoral
cooperation, increased social participation, and the creation of strong new environmental
standards. The primary difference between this law and the previous Ley Federal de Proteccion
al Ambiente of 1981 (Federal Environmental Protection Law) is the multi-tiered nature of the
institutional framework it establishes. The new law is also considerably stronger with regard to
wildlife conservation and management of natural protected areas. Annex 1.1 provides a summary
of the General Ecology Law.
2.15   Institutionally, the General Ecology Law defines the areas under federal responsibility and
leaves responsibility for other areas to the states. This approach contrasts with the earlier law
which limited state and municipal responsibility to a few defined areas and reserved the rest to the
federal government. Among the responsibilities reserved for the federal government under this
new law are: to establish federal environmental standards, to evaluate environmental impacts of
major projects (including federal public works and most large industrial activities), to carry out
environmental protection actions in areas under federal jurisdiction (e.g. federal protected areas),
and to create and manage federal natural protected areas. State and local governments have
responsibility in such areas as regulating and controlling water pollution within the jurisdiction of
the state, verifying vehicle emissions, operating air quality monitoring systems, routine
monitoring and enforcement of regulations and standards for water pollution from municipal
sewage systems, regulating and disposing of solid wastes, and creating and managing state or
municipal natural protected areas.
2.16   To date, the federal government has published four regulations to the General Ecology
Law, in the areas of: (i) environmental impact assessment; (ii) hazardous wastes; (iii) prevention
and control of mobile source pollution in the Mexico City Metropolitan Area; and (iv) prevention
and control of atmospheric pollution. The government is in the final stages of preparing a
regulation in the area of prevention and control of water pollution. Prior to loan effectiveness,
this regulation will be issued and made effective (para. 4.3 (c)). The federal govermnent has
published some 50 technical standards in the areas of air and water pollution and hazardous
wastes. Despite this progress i2 consolidating the regulatory framework at the federal level, there
remain regulatory gaps and considerable work still needs to be done in preparing technical
standards in many areas. In addition, it is also necessary to review the legal framework to
identify and resolve conflicts between the General Ecology Law and other legislation, particularly
with regard to agrarian reform, rural development and public works. Annex 1.2 contains a list of
regulations and technical standards already established, and Annex 2.1 provides a list of additional
technical standards to be issued in the areas of wastewater, atmospheric pollution, solid and
hazardous waste, and environmental impact and risk assessment. The proposed project would
finance the technical studies necessary to support the preparation of over 100 technical standards
(para. 3.46).



-6-
2.17   At the state level, since 1988, 27 of Mexico's 31 states and territories have promulgated
their own environmental laws complementing the General Ecology Law. It is expected that within
the next few years all states will have passed environmental legislation. No state has yet
established its own technical standards in any area (states may only do so if the norms are at least
as strict as those of the federal government).
B.2   Instutional Setting
2.18   Ederal leve. Most federal responsibilities in the environmental sector fall within the
jurisdiction of the S  ria adeDes     Ltano en Ecologia (SEDUE). The Comision Nacional
dp Agjja (CNA) is responsible for managing water resources and surveilling effluent discharges
from municipal wastewater treatment plants and agricultural activities. The Secretaria de
Agricutura y REcgrsos Hidraulico (SARH) was responsible for managing federal natural
protected areas until 1985, when this responsibility was transferred to SEDUE. SARH retains
responsibility for soil conservation, reforestation, and regulating pesticide use (in coordination
with SEDUE and the Secretar ia  Salud. The Srtaria de Salud coordinates with SEDUE and
other federal secretariats as necessary in the area of environmental health (e.g. wastewater
treatment and use, studies of enivironmental impact on health, and regulation of hazardous
substances). In order to improve coordination between federal secretariats, and between the
federal and state and local governments, the General Ecology Law established the Comision
Nacional de E.ologgi (CONADE). CONADE, under the leadership of SEDUE/SE, analyzes
environmental issues and proposes priority programs and actions for consideration by the relevant
secretariats.
2.19   SEDUE is composed of three operational sub-secretariats: (i) urban development, (ii)
housing, and (iii) ecology. Within the Sub-Secretariat for Ecology (SEDUE/SE) there are four
General Directorates as follows:
(i)  the Direeco General de NLormatividad y Reeulacion Ecologica(GN ),wihs
(i)~ ~ c nG          th    _ ( DGNR                   E     ) , which is
responsible for preparing regulations and technical norms, reviewing environmental
impact and risk assessments of investment operations, and coordinating ecological
planning and zoning;
(ii)  the D1ie_ion Genal de Prevencion v Control de la Contaminacion Ambiental
(DGPCCA), which is responsible for monitoring compliance with air, water and soil
pollution standards;
(iii)  the X ion Genel de Conserva_ion Ecologica de los Recursos Naturabe (DGCERN),
which is responsible for managing the federal system of protected areas and for
monitoring compliance with legislation regarding wildlife protection; and
(iv)  the Direccion General de Promocion Ambital y  _Participacion Comunitaria (DGPAPC),
which is responsible for environmental education and community participation in
environmental protection.
2.20   SEDUE/SE has faced serious limitations in the past in carrying out its mandate under
Mexicani law due to insufficient technical and support staff, lack of office (information and



-7-
conmmunications) equipment, inadequate administrative manuals and procedures, and over-
centralization of decision-making. The leanest year for SEDUE/SE was 1989, after a steady
decline in resources between 1986 and 1989 and followed by an appreciable increase in 1990 and
1991. Between 1986 and 1989, SEDUE/SE's operational budget declined 60 percent in real
terms and salaries for higher level permanent staff fell 50 percent. SEDUE/SE lost approximately
25 percent of its permanent and fixed term staff; 20 percent of its telephones; and 35 percent of
its functioning vehicles. The 1990 operational budget was 30 percent higher in real terms than the
1989 level. As a result of its still precarious state, except for a core of experienced and dedicated
higher level staff, SEDUE/SE has difficulty retaining qualified staff. Because of its low pay
scales, SEDUE/SE tends to hire and train individuals with little prior experience in the sector;
once trained, they promptly leave SEDUE/SE for higher paying jobs in the private sector. The
government recognizes SEDUE/SE's budget-related problems, and is steadily increasing the
resources available to SEDUE/SE for environmental protection and natural resources
management. Nevertheless, it will take considerable additional budget increases in real terms to
enable SEDUE/SE to recover from its deteriorated state and to build up sufficient capacity to
perform its critical normative and coordination functions under the more decentralized approach
envisioned under the General Ecology Law. Strengthening SEDUE/SE to play a leadership role
in tackling Mexico's difficult environmental challenges would be the primary focus of the
proposed project. The project would support the creation of an inter-secretarial task force to
address the issues of sector financing and the use of economic instruments (para. 3.39), and the
annual review process would include monitoring the adequacy of the budget (para. 3.75).
2.21   SEDUE is represented in each state by a delegate and an under-delegate for ecology, with
operational units responsible for inspecting industry to ensure compliance with emission standards,
monitoring water and air quality, supervising management of federal protected areas, and
enforcing laws on wildlife protection. The delegations too continue to suffer from severe
financial limitations which limit the effectiveness with which they are able to function. They lack
sufficient technical and support staff, office equipment, field equipment and vehicles, and access
to adequate laboratory facilities to monitor effectively compliance with emission standards and
ambient air œmd water quality. Annex 1.4 contains organizational charts of SEDUE and a typical
state delegat. n.
2.22 State level. As noted in paragraph 2.17, 27 of Mexico's states have passed environmental
legislation (the first was Queretero in mid-1988). The institutional arrangements set up under
these laws vary, and state agencies have only recently begun to function. The states of Queretero
and Nuevo Leon are the farthest along in setting up their environmental authorities and several
programs are now well underway. The state of Jalisco, on the other hand, has only just begun.
As with other states, SEDUE's delegation in the state is working closely with the state
government to establish the state authority and train its staff. Clearly, for SEDUE/SE to
decentralize many of its functions in the manner prescribed under the General Ecology Law,
considerable work remains to be done to enable the states to take on these new functions.
SEDUE/SE is *n the best position to assist the states in increasing their capacity to play their
prescribed role, but because SEDUE/SE itself has been chronically underfunded, the agency has
had to devote its efforts to urgent routine functions and has only limited experience in providing
technical assistance to its counterparts at the state level. While the proposed project would initiate
pilot programs to strengthen a limited number of states, these issues could only be addressed more
fully under a possible follow-up project.



-8-
C.    Kae Government Sectoral Functions
C.1   Pollution Monitoring and Control
2.23   WgVteruality. Under Mexican law, SEDUE/SE is responsible for setting and ensuring
compliance with water quality standards. In order to evaluate the effectiveness of water pollution
control programs, SEDUE/SE has established a nationwide network for monitoring water quality,
comprised of 402 water quality monitoring stations around the country in rivers, lakes, reservoirs,
estuaries, beaches, ports and underground aquifers. Site selection for monitoring stations is based
on proximity to pollution sources; waterworks for municipal, industrial and agricultural uses;
fisheries and recreatior areas; important ecosystems and natural protected areas; as well as the
representativeness of the receiving body of water, ease of access and collection of samples. and
proximity to stations for analyzing water samples. SEDUE/SE's monitoring plan calls for
collecting samples from these stations each month and analyzing 20 basic water quality parameters
including ambient temperature, dissolved oxygen, pH, conductivity, BOD/COD, suspended
particulates, sulfates and fecal coliforms. Due to insufficient resources (material, equipment and
trained personnel) this monitoring system is not yet functioning adequately. The proposed project
would support SEDUE/SE's monitoring system and training of its personnel (para. 3.8).
2.24   CNA also operates monitoring stations around the country in order to verify the quality
(and quantity) of available water. In some cases the two institutions' collect samples from nearby
points, although the agencies collect the samples for different purposes and carry out different
analyses. Nevertheless, there is room for increasing the efficiency of collection and analysis of
water samples by coordinating the location of sampling stations and the parameters analyzed, as
well as unifying data bases containing water quality information. SEDUE/SE and CNA recognize
this and have already begun discussing how best to coordinate their efforts. The proposed project
would support the redesign of the national water quality monitoring system to be managed jointly
by SEDUE and CNA (para. 3.8).
2.25   Air qualitv. SEDUE/SE is responsible for setting and enforcing air quality standards and
for maintaining a national information system. In the Federal District, SEDUE/SE is also
responsible for controlling fixed-source atmospheric pollution, and for establishing and operating
an air pollution monitoring system. SEDUE/SE currently operates a National Information System
on Stationary Sources (SNIFF), which currently contains data on only 5 percent of the nearly
135,000 small, medium and large stationary sources of atmospheric pollution in Mexico. Outside
of the Federal District, responsibility for monitoring air quality lies with state governments, who
may seek assistance from SEDUE/SE and must submit plans for establishing systems to monitor
air quality to SEDUE/SE for technical approval. States (and the authorities of the Federal
District) are also responsible for verifying vehicle emissions. The Federal District and a few
states (such as Queretero, Nuevo Leon and Jalisco) have made progress in establishing vehicle
emissions laboratories and air quality monitoring systems. Much work remains to be done,
however, and states will require considerable technical assistance from SEDUE/SE to plan and
establish their monitoring systems. The proposed project would support SEDUE/SE's efforts to
provide this assistance (para. 3.10)
2.26   Eonmental laboratories. SEDUE/SE presently maintains one central laboratory, 10
regional laboratories and 21 state level laboratories. State level laboratories carry out basic



-9-
analyses of water quality; regional laboratories are capable of carrying out slightly more complex
analyses of air, water, soil and solid wastes. The central laboratory, located in Mexico City,
carries out complex analyses and provides technical support to the entire system. Many of these
laboratories are operating In precarious conditions, with inadequate space, old equipment and
insufficient trained technical and support personnel.
2.27   Consistent with SEDUE/SE's plan to decentralize routine monitoring and enforcement
functions to the state level as states increase their capacity to handle this new responsibility,
SEDUE/SE places highest priority on consolidating and strengthening its central and regional
laboratories. Over the medium-term, SEDUE/SE's laboratory facilities in the states would be
phased out. This phasing out would be coordinated with the development and certification of
capacity at the state level and in the private sector, which would be supported under the proposed
project (para. 3.14).
2.28   Inspection and vigilance. Under various of its responsibilities as defined in the General
Ecology Law, SEDUE/SE must maintain a workforce of inspectors in the Federal District and
each of the states. These inspectors carry out routine inspections and investigate complaints,
identify infractions of existing standards and the measures that must be taken by non-complying
firms to comply with the standard, and conduct all follow-up inspections as necessary to ensure
compliance with agreements signed by SEDUE/SE and individual firms.
2.29  In the Mexico City Metropolitan Area alone, SEDUE/SE is responsible for inspecting
approximately 15,000 firms. In order to carry out this task in the metropolitan area, SEDUE/SE
has only 9 trained inspectors (3 for wastewater, 4 for atmospheric emissions, and 2 for noise) and
can mobilize another 8 lesser trained staff on an emergency basis. Throughout the rest of the
country, SEDUE/SE has 66 inspectors to cover some 40,000 firms. This number of inspectors is
clearly insufficient and the government has recently taken steps to increase the number of
inspectors. The proposed project would support SEDUE/SE's efforts to increase the number of
trained inspectors and to use their expertise more effectively to audit emissions reports submitted
by the firms themselves (para. 3.14).
C.2   Minimizing Negative Environmental Impacts of Investment Projects
2.30   Environmental impact and risk assessment. The General Ecology Law requires all public
or private sector activities and works that could potentially cause significant environmental
disturbance or exceed limits or conditions established in environmental regulations or technical
norms to receive prior authorization from the responsible level of government. If approved
conditionally, the proponent must demonstrate that all conditions have been met. In the case of
works or activities considered to be of high risk, the proponent must also present SEDUE/SE with
a risk assessment when requesting authorization.
2.31   Shortly after the General Ecology Law went into effect, SEDUE published a regulation on
enviromnental impact assessments, which defined specific areas where SEDUE must review an
impact assessment, procedures for preparing and presenting impact assessments (including
publication for public review), and sanctions for failing to comply with the regulation. On the
basis of the proponent's "general" impact assessment, or in some cases, manifestation that the
proposed project would not cause an environmental disturbance or exceed limits or conditions



- 10-
established in environmental regulations or technical norms, SEDUE/SE determines whether
additional information is required in the form of an "intermediate" or "specific" impact
assessment. On the basis of its review of the proponent's documentation, SEDUE may approve
the activity unconditionally, approve the activity with conditions, or reject the activity.
2.32   SEDUE/SE currently reviews about 600 impact assessments (of all three types) and 200
risk assessments each year. Within SEDUE/SE, it is the DGNRE that is responsible for carrying
out the review, with technical input from the DGCERN and the DGPCCA when appropriate. In
light of the fact that environmental assessments are relatively recent in Mexico, their quality is
often inadequate and SEDUE/SE frequently must request additional complementary information.
At the same time, SEDUE/SE is understaffed to carry out its own review function adequately,
including field visits and follow-up. In order to cope with its workload, SEDUE/SE must also
strengthen the staff in its state delegations to assume greater responsibility for reviewing
enviromnental assessments, and provide adequate supervision and technical support from the
central offices. The proposed project would support an action plan to strengthen SEDUE's state
delegations in this regard, and would strengthen SEDUE's central offices to review risk
assessments and complex impact assessments (para. 3.17).
2.33   Ecological Pianning and ZoninZ. Another strategy that SEDUE/SE is following in order
to facilitate the review of environmental assessments in the future is to work with public sector
agencies to carry out systematic ecological planning and zoning exercises prior to beginning large
investment projects (sometimes multi-sectoral) in a given geographic area. SEDUE/SE has
received from each federal sectoral agency a list of priority investment projects through the end of
1994. By locating each of these projects on a map, SEDUE/SE was able to identify areas that are
likely to come under increased environmental pressure over the next few years. Based on this
analysis, SEDUE/SE then proposed to the sector agencies involved to carry out ecological studies
and ecological planning and zoning exercises prior to finalizing the investment project. As a
general principle, the sector agencies are expected to bear the costs of carrying out the studies as
part of project preparation costs. SEDUE/SE's role is to assist the sector agencies by preparing
terms of reference, identifying consultants, and reviewing outputs. Finally, SEDUE/SE will
consolidate all of the information from the studies into a geographic information system (para.
3.21).
C.3   Conservation of Biodiversity
2.34 bistema Nacional de Areas Protegidas (SINAP). To protect its rich biological wealth,
Mexico has established a national system of protected areas, SINAP. Annex 1.5 lists the nine
management categories of these areas and describes their management objectives. Administration
of the SINAP is the responsibility of the DGCERN within SEDUE/SE.
2.35   By the mid-1980s, this system included over a hundred protected areas. A recent re-
evaluation of the biological value of these areas recognized that many were too small to be viable
over the long term or had become too degraded due to inadequate protection. Currently,
Mexico's SINAP consists of 66 natural protected areas, legally denominated as follows: 44
National Parks, 8 Biosphere Reserves, 13 Special Biosphere Reserves, and one Area for the
Protection of Flora and Fauna. The SINAP encompasses 5.5 million hectares, constituting almost
3 percent of the national territory (compared with 2.4 percent in Brazil, 4.0 percent in Argentina,



- I1 -
5.4 percent in Colombia, 8.6 percent in the USA, and 12.0 percent in Costa Rica). The proposed
project would support the identification of gaps in the habitats protected by the SINAP and the
possible expansion of the system (para. 3.49).
2.36   While the steady progress that the Mexican government has made towards the
establishment of a comprehensive network of protected areas is encouraging, the task of
identification and protection of critical habitats is not yet complete. Many existing areas are
seriously threatened by uncontrolled exploitation and population expansion into critical habitats,
often resulting in ecosystem deterioration and loss of biodiversity. During the fiscal contraction
of the 1980s, as the Mexican government attempted to stabilize the economy and put the country
back on the path of steady economic growth, the protected areas received inadequate staffing and
budgets, severely limiting the capability of managers to respond to threats to conservation.
Appropriate management and zoning of the protected areas is critical since the majority of these
areas contain private and communal lands (ejidos) which subject the protected areas to pressures
for the use of their natural resources.
2.37   Mexico is now seeking to strengthen its capacity to protect its biodiversity resources,
taking into account its limited resources and the need to set priorities carefully. The country's
strategy emphasizes the need to strike a balance between conservation and exploitation of the
resources contained in its protected areas. This balance is achieved through careful planning,
culminating in the preparation and implementation of a management plan for each protected area.
Based on a detailed analysis of the physical and social characteristics of the protected area, the
management plan specifies the specific objectives to be achieved in the area, and the regulations
and programmatic activities necessary to ensure the achievement of those objectives. This
approach is described in the document, "Protected Natural Areas of Mexico: Legislation and
Management Plans" (Translation), transmitted to the Bank by SEDUE's Under-secretary for
Ecology (Annex 1.9). This document would provide the basis for support, under the proposed
project (with financing from the Global Environment Trust Fund -- GET), for the effective
management of up to 17 high-priority protected areas (para. 3.24). In addition, some of the
country's biologically richest areas are still not protected at all, and the productivity of the
protected areas could be enhanced through the establishment of biological corridors which allow
gene and species recruitment between areas. The proposed project would support a set of studies
to help prepare a strategy and regulations for the establishment of biological corridors (para.
3.52).
2.38   Wildlife Trade. Mexican conservation authorities also face the task of controlling
significant illegal trade in wildlife. This trade, spurred largely by demand for pets and
ornamental plants abroad, represents a tremendous drain on the countries' flora and fauna.
Parrots, sea turtles, cacti, iguanas and caimans are among the favored species, despite Mexico's
outright prohibition of or strict restrictions against their trade. In fact, Mexico's strict law goes
as far as to prohibit all commercial import and export of live wildlife and pets that are native
species, as well as the export of wildlife products. Excepted from those prohibitions are wildlife
parts (such as leathers) from non-endangered species and from animals bred in captivity. Given
the importance of Mexico's shores as a breeding ground for most of the world's species of marine
turtles, it is particularly important to protect these breeding grounds from poaching. Mexico
recently signed the Convention on International Trade in Endangered Species of Wild Fauna and
Flora (CITES) which provides and international framework and support network for controlling
trade in endangered species. The proposed project would support the government's capability to



- 12 -
fulfill its commitment to control trade in endangered species (para. 3.29).
D.    Goyernmegnt Strategy for the Future
2.39   The government's priorities for environmental policies and actions, as summarized in the
1989-94 National Development Plan, are: (i) to strengthen the legal framework to prevent
deterioration of the environment, and use environmental regulation as an efficient instrument of
environmental protection of the country's geographical zones and regions; (ii) to subject all
economic activities and projects to strict criteria of environmental care; (iii) to design policies and
take actions to prevent further deterioration of, and improve, air and water quality, prevent or
slow down soil erosion, and introduce adequate treatment of solid wastes and hazardous materials;
and (iv) to increase public awareness of, and participation in, environmental protection.
2.40   The legal and institutional framework for environmental protection and natural resources
management is now well established in Mexico. In early 1990, SEDUE published the "National
Program for Environmental Protection: 1990-1994. " This program is intended to orient the
efforts of the federal government and social groups in environmental protection. Key emphases
of the program (described in greater detail in Annex 1.3) are: (i) decentralization of
environmental control authority to state and municipal levels of government; (ii) ecological
planning and zoning, including appropriate designation of wildlands as natural protected areas;
(iii) employment of the "Polluter Pays Principle" to ensure that development decisions internalize
associated environmental costs, and to help defray the cost of environmental management; and
(iv) interagency coordination.
2.41   With regard to SEDUE/SE's own role in the sector, it is apparent that the agency cannot
continue to attempt as it has in the past to carry out front-line environmental protection functions.
For example, if SEDUE/SE inspectors were to attempt to visit each year all of the 15,000 firms
in the Mexico City Metropolitan Area that fall under SEDUE/SE's jurisdiction, the agency would
need an workforce of about 600 inspectors in the metropolitan area alone;3 to visit all 40,000
firms around the country, SEDUE/SE would need about three times that number. Of course the
agency would also need to hire additional supervisors and support staff, and increase its physical
facilities and laboratory and legal support. The fiscal costs of attempting effectively to follow this
approach would be very substantial.
2.42   Rather than to continue to function as a front-line enforcement agency, however,
SEDUE/SE plans to adopt the approach of other industrialized countries -- that is, to require
firms to monitor their own emissions, to audit firms' self-monitoring reports (randomly and based
on other environmental quality data that SEDUE/SE must collect), and to impose stiff penalties if
reports are found to have been falsified. In order for SEDUE/SE to adopt this more modem
approach in all areas of environmental protection and natural resources management within its
mandate, SEDUE/SE plans to transform itself into a second tier institution - a relatively small
agency with a highly trained and experienced staff that supervises and coordinates the efforts of
other federal and state agencies, and of the private sector, through a combination of technical
3Assuming that inspectors working in teams of three could visit and follow up on about 80
firms each year.



- 13 -
assistance, and regulatory and market-based instruments. SEDUE/SE's strategy in this regard is
contained in a letter from the government (see para. 3.4 and Annex 1.6).
2.43   Within SEDUE/SE, the beginnings of this strategy are already apparent. In the area of
environmental impact assessment, SEDUE/SE has been working with the sectoral secretariats of
the federal government to carry out on ecological planning and zoning studies in geographic areas
where these secretariats plan major investments over the next few years. SEDUE/SE's role is to
assist the sectoral secretariats to identify study needs, prepare terms of reference for consultants to
carry out the studies, and review the studies' findings. All costs of the studies are borne by the
sectoral secretariat itself as normal costs of project preparation. With regard to monitoring air
and water quality, SEDUE/SE has decided to invest heavily in strengthening central and regional
laboratories, and to rely on private laboratory services for basic analyses. SEDUE/SE and CNA
are working to harmonize their water quality information systems, coordinating collection and
analysis of samples as well as developing a single data base for storage and retrieval of water
quality data. Finally, SEDUE/SE is moving toward giving greater responsibility to industry to
monitor emissions, with SEDUE/SE's inspectors conducting environmental audits to verify self-
monitoring reports.
E.    Rationale for Bank Involvement
2.44   The Bank's assistance program for Mexico is designed to support the recovery of
economic growth, and places special emphasis on strengthening the social sectors and on
protecting the environment. Within the environmental sector, the focus is on efforts to improve
resource use and productivity, as well as to ensure that policy analysis evaluates the social costs
(including environmental impacts) of potential investments and policies. Given the weaknesses of
the environmental sector the Bank's resources and presence are needed to help catalyze
administrative reform, institutional strengthening, and policy development in such areas as the
efficient use of regulation and the application of the polluter pays principle.
2.45   The proposed project would complement on-going and proposed projects in the
agriculture, infrastructure and industry sectors, which tackle the environmental problems
discussed in Section 2A through the sectoral secretariats. These operations rely on an adequate
institutional set-up in the environmental sector to ensure compliance with existing environmental
norms. (Mhe legal and regulatory framework and the institutional responsibility for policy making
and implementation of environmental protection and natural resource management are described in
Section B.) The Forestry Development Project (Ln. 3115-ME) aims at supporting sustainable
forestry and includes resources for SEDUE/SE to prepare management plans for protected areas
in the two states in the project area. The Water and Sanitation Sector Project (Ln. 3271-ME) and
the Decentralization and Regional Development Project (Ln. 3310-ME) also have environment
components working with CNA and environmental agencies in four states, respectively. The
Mining Sector Restructuring Project (Ln. 3359-ME) includes support for enacting industry-
specific environmental standards for the mining sector. The Irrigation and Drainage Sector
Project (Ln. 3419) supports CNA in strengthening adherence to environmental safeguards in
irrigation projects. A proposed Transport Air Pollution Project (PY92) in the Mexico City
Metropolitan Area would assist Mexico in reducing air pollution from mobile sources in the
MlCMA. A proposed Agricultural Technology Project (FY92) would support the efforts of the
extension service to stem on-farm soil erosion. In addition, a work program and projects aimed



- 14 -
at phasing out the use of ozone depleting substances are at an advanced stage of preparation and
would be financed on concessional terms under the provisions of the Montreal Protocol.
2.46   Batignale for Involvem¢nt of the lobal Environment Facilitv (GEF). Mexico's SINAP
contains species and ecosystems of global importance. Given the urgency to of addressing critical
air and water pollution problems, the government is not able to provide SINAP with the level of
resources needed to ensure that this important biodiversity is protected. In light of heavy
development pressures on protected areas, if management of these areas is not improved and
additional resources quickly allocated to protection activities, the potential near-term losses in
species, both in variety and in uniqueness, are vast. The proposed protected areas program would
ensure effective protection and management of up to 17 important protected areas covering 80
percent of SINAP's land area. It would also yield lessons that would be applicable to other
countries' situations. An allocation of US$30 million from the GET is justified because: (i) the
program would allow the testing in several ecosystems of a variety of approaches to protecting
biodiversity in tlhe context of specific challenges to the viability of individual protected areas; and
(ii) the approach to financing (i.e. a time-slice type operation) if found successful, could be used
in other countries where the legal and institutional conditions would permit a flexible project
design and allow the rapid absorption of a relatively large amount of resources.
F.    Lessons from Experience
2.47   By focusing support on Mexico's central environmental agency, the project would
complement the Bank's efforts in environmental protection in other sectoral projects. It would
address important institutional weaknesses of SEDUE/SE and regulatory gaps identified in the
context of the recent Project Completion Report for the Mexico Pollutien Control Project (Ln.
2154-ME). A major lesson of that project and industrial pollution control projects elsewhere is
the importance of strong capacity to enforce pollution standards and to provide the proper
incentives for polluters to invest in pollution control equipment. The proposed project is also
designed to relieve a critical bottleneck to implementation of environmentally sound investment
projects in Mexico caused by SEDUE/SE's currently weak capacity to review the volume of
environmental impact assessments required under Mexican law. This problem has been
highlighted in supervision reports of a number of on-going projects. The proposed project would
support the initial phase of SEDUE/SE's strategy to decentralize (to SEDUE's state delegations)
its workload in reviewing environmental impact assessments, and increase the agency's technical
and managerial capacity to carry out the reviews effectively and efficiently.
2.48   A recent review of supervision experience with several Bank-financed projects in Mexico
with environmental components indicated the following major implementation difficulties:
o     Due to budgetary reductions in the mid-1980s, SEDUE/SE has had limited technical and
financial capacity to set and enforce technical standards. Staff turnover in all areas is
high, and SEDUE/SE's small, poorly paid staff of inspectors has had difficulties in
enforcing industry compliance with established technical standards. The proposed project
would support a strategy of transforming SEDUE/SE's staff into a better remunerated
cadre of qualified, experienced and motivated environmental managers.
o     Institutional coordination for environmental protection has been limited. In particular,



- 15 -
until recently, the roles and responsibilities of SEDUE/SE and CNA have been unclear,
and there has been some duplication of effort and inter-agency rivalry. The project would
support the establistiment of a single national water quality monitoring network,
harmonization of sample collection and analysis methodologies among SEDUE/SE and
CNA, and development of a single data base of water quality indicators. In parallel with
the approach pursued under the Irrigation and Drainage Project, SEDUE and SARH
would sign a coordination agreement detailing their respective roles and responsibilities
for the national water quality monitoring system and for coordination of their
environmental laboratories (para. 3.9).
O     Environmental components of Bank-financed projects have suffered chronic delays in the
provision of counterpart funds. Nevertheless, political commitment to the proposed
project appears strong. SEDUE/SE received a major budgetary increase in 1991 to begin
carrying out the proposed project. To assure timely provision of adequate counterpart
funds throughout project implementation, allocation by GOM of a suitable budget for
1992, satisfactory to the Bank would be a condition of effectiveness (paras. 3.65 and 4.3
(a)). The adequacy of implementing agency budgets in future years would be addressed
in the context of the annual review meetings (paras. 3.75 and 4.2 (1)).
2.49   With regard to the experience of free-standing environmental projects, there is only one
other such project in the Latin America and the Caribbean Region --'the "Brazil - Nationai
Environmental Project" (Ln. 3173-BR), which became effective in December 1990. Early
experience in implementation of this project underlines the importance of building flexibility into
the project design (e.g., through annual review and planning exercises at which design changes
can be agreed). Experience also indicates that Bank supervision requirements are likely to be
higher than average for Bank-financed projects.
2.50   A general lesson from other investment projects with relatively weak institutions is the
advisability of focusing efforts on a few line ministries. This simplifies implementation
arrangements and Bank supervision, allowing the government and the Bank to place greater
emphasis on technical rather than procedural issues. It also increases accountability within the
government for progress on project implementation. Another lesson is the need to specify
speciflc outputs that allow the government and the Bank to assess periodically the degree to which
the project is achieving its stated goals. By holding periodic reviews on progress in achieving the
expected outputs, the government and the Bank have the opportunity to make any necessary
modifications in project design at an early date. The proposed project design incorporates both of
these lessons.
III. THlE PROJECT
A.    Origin of the Project
3.1    The proposed project grew out of the increased emphasis that the federal fivernment has
placed on environmental protection and natural resource management especially over the past two
years. In the context of this increased emphasis (manifest in rapidly growing current and capital
budgets for SEDUE/SE), the federal government realized the need to strengthen the institutional
framework of the sector to prepare the federal agency to take on additional responsibilities. In



- 16 -
July 1990, the government requested a technical assistance loan from the Bank to help identify
and prepare Investment projects in the environment sector. By September 1990, the government
decided on a two-pronged approach with an investment project that would finance: (i) key
Investments in areas where SEDUE/SE already has sufficient implementation capacity, and (ii)
technical assistance in areas where SEDUE/SE needs further strengthening in preparation for
developing and implementing future investments. This two-pronged approach has guided
preparation of the proposed project.
B.    Pr-ojegt Objective
3.2    The overall objective of the proposed project would be to support the government's ability
to carry out key environmental protection and natural resource management functions in the short
term and at the same time to strengthen the institutional and policy framework to enable the
government to carry out these functions in a more efficient and decentralized manner in the
future. The project would support the government's strategy of transforming SEDUE/SE over
time into a small but highly qualified second tier organization that supervises and coordinates
environmental work carried out by other levels of government or other federal agencies, or under
contract to private sector firms.
3.3    The project's specific objectives would be to: (i) support action in key areas where pre-
investment work is already well developed and institutional absorptive capacity is adequate; (ii)
reform and strengthen the administrative structure of SEDUE's federal and state offices in
preparation for increased decentralization of responsibility and authority to state environmental
agencies over the medium term; and (iii) support development of a sectoral strategy and coherent
policy framework that is integrated into and consistent with the government's overall economic
policies.
3.4   Programs and activities under the key functions component would provide the setting for
defining in concrete terms some of the issues of institutional structure and policy addressed under
components dealing specifically with strengthening the institutional and policy framework. Most
of the activities under the key functions component would be implemented at the level of
SEDUE's delegations in the states, who in turn would in some cases contract with private sector
firms, consistent with the agency's policy of shifting federal operational authority and
responsibility to its state offices and relying to a greater extent on the private sector. The
Mexican government has sent the Bank a letter describing its strategy to transform SEDUE/SE
into a second tier institution (Annex 1.6). This letter describes SEDUE/SE's functions at the
central and state delegation levels, and several activities that have already been identified to be
contracted out to the private sector. It emphasizes the government's intention to incorporate the
poliluter pays principle into environmental policy design.
C.    Project Description
3.5    The project would finance the first phase of the government's environmental program
concentrating on key functions of SEDUE/SE and setting the stage for a more decentralized
approach, plus activities and studies to support preparation of a second phase which would focus
on state-level environmental agencies, building on the experience of the first phase. The first



- 17-
phase would have three components Implemented over a four-year period. Component I would
finance key environmental services, namely monitoring and controlling water and air pollution
(29% of total project costs), minimizing potential negative environmental impacts of public and
private sector investment projects (11%) and conserving biodiversity (44%). Co£ponentIl would
strengthen sector management and administration, through institutional strengthening of
SEDUE/SE (4%), a pilot decentralization program with up to five states (5%), and the
commissioning of special studies to explore the use of economic instruments to complement the
current heavily command and control approach to environmental management (19%). Component
i would support the strengthening of the regulatory framework through the preparation of
additional technical standards for pollution control and natural resource protection (3%).
Preparation for the second phase of the government's sectoral program would involve carrying
out four pre-investment studies and financing the activities of a Project Coordination Unit (PCU;
see para. 3.72) reporting to SEDUE/SE to prepare the program (3 %).
C.1   Key Functions Component
3.6    Under the key functions component, the project would support SEDUE/SE's efforts in the
central and state offices in three critical areas: (i) monitoring and control of water and air
pollution; (ii) minimizing negative environmental impacts of investment projects; and (iii)
conserving biodiversity.
3.7    Monitoring and Control of Pollution. The specific objective of this sub-component would
be to increase the efficiency and effectiveness of SEDUE/SE's efforts to monitor pollution and
enforce existing pollution control laws and regulations. To achieve this objective, the sub-
component would support three programs: (i) water pollution; (ii) air pollution; and (iii)
environmental laboratories.
3.8   WATER POLLUTION PROGRAM (US$16.2 million in total costs). The specific
objective of the water pollution program would be to augment SEDUE/SE's capacity to monitor
compliance with industrial and municipal effluent standards, and to monitor the quality of
receiving water bodies (in coordination with CNA). To accomplish this objective, the project
would support: (i) the redesign of the national water quality monitoring system jointly with
CNA) and operation of SEDUE/SE's portion of the system (Annex 2.2); (ii) the upgrading of
SEDUE/SE's staff responsible for water pollution control through formal and on-the-job training,
and by working in close collaboration with universities, research centers, and the private sector
(Annex 3.3); and (iii) a series of studies to bring SEDUE/SE's information base up-to-date and
provide its staff with new tools (mathematical models, geographic information systems, statistical
data analysis software, integrated data bases) (Annex 3.2).
3.9   SEDUE and CNA have agreed to coordinate their respective efforts in water quality
monitoring and control of discharge of liquid effluents into water bodies. This agreement is
registered in a coordination agreement, satisfactory to the Bank, signed by the heads of SEDUE
and SARH - the Secretariat to which the head of CNA is responsible (Annex 1.7 -- translation).
A ilegoiatigns, the government provided assurances that no modifications will be made to this
coordination agreement without the prior agreement of the Bank (para. 4.2 (c)). With regard to
water quality monitoring, the two agencies will establish a single water quality data base and
operate a common quality assurance program for the data collection process. Terms of reference



- 18 -
for the redesign of the national water quality monitoring system operated by SEDUE/SE and
CNA are contained in Annex 2.2. In ^-'dition, the two agencies have agreed to coordinate their
respective networks of environmental laboratories to take advantage of each agency's regional
laboratories (para. 3.12).
3.10   AIR POLLUTION PROGRAM (US$6.9 million). The principal objectives of the air
pollution program would be to strengthen the infrastructure and human resources to monitor air
quality in 36 cities with populations of more than 50,000, and enforce compliance with stationary
source air emission standards. The program would involve the installation of new and
improvement of existing air quality monitoring networks, and the updating and operation of the
National Information System for Stationary Sources (SNIFF). During the first two years of the
project, SEDUE/SE would supplement the 35 existing air quality monitoring stations with 19
meteorological and 80 stations to monitor NO2, SO2 and fine particulates (PM1O).
3.11  In order to carry out the program, SEDUE/SE has divided the country into six regions,
based on geographical, ciimatological, demographic and economic characteristics. Regional
centers would be located in the cities of Tijuana, Chihuahua, Monterrey, Guadalajara, Puebla and
Villahermosa. The project would increase SEDUEiSE's staff located in the six regions, train the
new staff through in-service training with current SEDUE/SE staff, and provide them with the
infrastructure and equipment necessary to measure stationary source emissions in the smokestack.
Consistent with SEDUE/SE's planned role as a second tier organization that coordinates the
efforts of others, SEDUE/SE has adopted the strategy of obliging major industrial polluters in
each city to acquire, install and operate (under SEDUE/SE's supervision) air quality monitoring
equipment. Where such arrangements are possible, SEDUE/SE's role is to design the network,
provide technical assistance as necessary, and monitor the results. SEDUE/SE integrates the data
collected from such networks in a single data base to be used for: (i) identifying inconsistencies
in self-monitoring reports leading to a subsequent audit; and (ii) providing a basis for new policies
and programs to bring air pollution down to socially acceptable levels.
3.12 ENVIRONMENTAL LABORATORIES PROGRAM (US$13.7 :nillion). The objective
of the environmental laboratories program would be to support SEDUE/SE's efforts in pollution
monitoring and control with investments in laboratory infrastructure and human resources. The
program would support the expansion and remodelling of the facilities and staff training at
SEDUE/SE's central environmental laboratory (in Mexico City) and in six regional laboratories
(in the cities of Chihuahua, Guanajuato, Mexicali, Saltillo, Villahermosa and Xalapa). Regional
laboratory facilities of SEDUE would complement CNA's regional laboratories. SEDUE's
laboratories are located in different cities, and the two agencies are currently making
arrangements to enable them to take advantage of each other's laboratory facilities whenever
possible.
3.13   SEDUE/SE's central laboratory would have the capability to carry out a complete range
of environmental analyses and would serve as a reference laboratory for SEDUE/SE's regional
laboratories. New equipment for the central laboratory would include a plasma emission
spectrophotometer (for metals analysis) and a gas chromatograph, mass spectrometer and liquid
chromatograph (for analysis of organic compounds). The regional laboratories would be equipped
to carry out routine analyses for toxic substances (metals and organic). The project would
strengthen the current laboratory quality control program in both its internal and external
components, including with CNA laboratories and other phases of the monitoring and surveillance



- 19 -
process (e.g., the field and data handling phases). Preparation of architectural and engineering
plans acceptable to the Bank would be a condition of disbursement for each laboratory (para. 4.5
(a)).
3.14   As described in the strategy letter from the Government (Annex 1.6), within the context
of SEDUE/SE's transformation into a second-tier organization, the private sector would
increasingly take over many of the routine data collection activities currently carried out by
SEDUE/SE. With regard to ensuring compliance with emission standards, industry would be
required to submit to SEDUE/SE periodic monitoring reports either prepared in-house or under
contract with private firms. SEDUE/SE's role would be to certify the private contractors and
audit industry self-monitoring reports to ensure their veracity. In order to develop this capability,
SEDUE/SE would increase its workforce of environmental inspectors who would receive formal
training and on-the-job training over the first two years from seasoned SEDUE/SE inspectors.
Formal training would include regional and national seminars where experts would be invited to
lecture on specialized technical topics. In the third and fourth year of the project, these
individuals wouid begin to function as environmental auditors. They would concentrate their
efforts on certifying companies who would carry out front-line monitoring and auditing these
companies and firms who conduct their own effluent monitoring. The project would support this
effort as well as the design and implementation of quality assurance activities for the whole
enforcement program. The precise design of the proposed approach is still in its formative stages
and would be furffier developed in the context of the institutional analysis to be conducted under
the modernization of environmental management component (paras. 3.35 and 3.36). SEDUE/SE
and the Bank would agree on the precise nature of the environmental auditing program during the
first annual review meeting (para. 3.75) (approx. February 1993).
3.15 Minimizing Negative Environmental Impacts of Investment Proiects. The environmental
impacts sub-component would support SEDUE/SE in increasing the efficiency and effectiveness of
the environmental impact and risk assessment process. It would support two programs: (i)
impact and risk assessment, and (ii) ecological ordering.
3.16 IMPACT AND RISK ASSESSMENT (US$6.6 million). The objective of the impact
and risk assessment program would be to reinforce SEDUE/SE's abilities (in the central office
and in SEDUE's state delegations) to review environmental impact and risk assessments, and to
monitor compliance with conditions imposed on approval of public and private sector investments
projects. The program would: (i) increase the number and technical capabilities of staff
responsible for reviewing environmental assessment documents; (ii) delegate to SEDUE's state
delegations the authority, and increase their technical capability, to evaluate and approve
environmental impact assessments; (iii) increase the capacity of state environmental authorities to
review environmental assessments in areas within their jurisdiction; and (iv) increase public access
and input into the environmental assessment review process.
3.17   Ihe government has defined a program acceptable to the Bank and in agreement with
Mexican legislation to transfer to SEDUE's state delegations the function of evaluating
environmental impact assessments and authorizing projects. Under this program, during the first
year of the project, SEDUE/SE would contract and train specialists to work in SEDUE's central
offices and state delegations. The number of new technical staff envisioned under the plan, which
reaches a total of 56 in year 3 of the project, is based on the anticipated number of document
reviews that will be required annually over the next four years. In order to reduce the current



- 20 -
bottleneck of environmental reviews at headquarters, SEDUE/SE has determined which types of
projects may be reviewed and approved by SEDUE's state delegations, with support and
supervision from headquarters' staff (Annex 1.8). At negotiations, the government and the Bank
agreed on: (i) the dates by which SEDUE will transfer authority to SEDUE's state delegatiors to
review environmental impact assessments in agreed sub-sectors and to monitor compliance with
established mitigation plans; and (ii) the staffing and training requirements of SEDUE's central
offices and state delegations for this purpose (para. 4.2 (d)).
3.18 In order to support SEDUE/SE's decision to delegate responsibility and authority to state
delegations, the project would include training for staff working in state delegations. SEDUE/SE
would first develop and test model training courses, which would then be offered to staff from all
of SEDUE's state delegations. After the test phase, the courses would be carried out by key staff
from SEDUE's headquarters with the assistance of specialized consultants and using methods and
materials (including videos, handbooks, slides, etc.) demonistrated to be effective in the pilot
phase. Due to the highly specialized nature of risk assessment, training in this area would occur
separately from training in environmental impact assessment.
3.19   Finally, the impact and risk assessment program would include, a study to determine how
to increase public input into the impact and risk assessment review process. The consultants
would analyze the current situation and recommend specific procedures to make documents more
readily available to the public within the time constraints established by SEDUE regulations.
Following completion of the consultants' study, SEDUE/SE would organize a one-day workshop
in Mexico City and invite representatives from all 31 states to discuss the proposed new
procedures that would later be formalized through publication in the Gaceta Ecologica. At
igegotiations, agreement was reached that new procedures, satisfactory to the Bank, would be
published by December 31, 1992 (para. 4.2 (d)).
3.20 ECOLOGICAL PLANNING AND ZONING PROGRAM (US$7.0 million). The
objective of this component is to integrate environmental protection objectives into planning
decisions on priority activities affecting regional economic production, to ensure that national
productive infrastructure will be compatible with environmental protection objectives. This
program would support the monitoring of compliance with the general ecological planning and
zoning study that is currently being carried out by the government and which will be completed
by early 1992. The objective of this general study would be to provide decision-makers and
Mexican society at-large with the information necessary to establish national policies of
protection, conservation, restoration or use of the land and natural resources in an
environmentally sustainable manner (see Annex 1.3 p.1, Annex 3.1, p. 13 and Annex 3.2, p.2).
Completion of the general ecological planning and zoning study would be a condition of
disbursements on expenditures to monitor compliance with the zoning exercise (para. 4.5 (c)).
3.21  In addition to the general ecological planning and zoning study, the component would
finance 20 environmental studies and 10 specific ecological planning and zoning studies in
selected geographic areas where multi-sectoral investments are planned (Annex 3.2, p. 2-5).
These studies have been chosen from a larger list of large investment projects received from each
federal agency. These studies would serve as models to develop appropriate methodologies for
future such studies. At negotiations, the govermnent confirmed that: (i) following completion of
