each of the 10 specific ecological planning and zoning studies, SEDUE/SE will formalize land-use
regulation of the project area in question by publishing a summary of the ecological planning and



-21 -
zoning study in the Gaceta Ecol6gica (Ecological Journal); and (ii) the government shall make
operational by iune 30, 1992, in a manner satisfactory to the Bank, a geographic information
system (GIS) to store the results of the environmental studies and the ecological planning and
zoning exercises (para. 4.2 (e)).
3.22   The ecological planning and zoning program would also include three 10-day training
courses (for 30 people each) to train staff from SEDUE's central offices and state delegations, as
well as participants from state agencies, in the policies and strategies of environmental studies and
ecological planning and zoning. In addition to these three courses, the project would provide
resources for the development and distribution of educational materials on environmental studies
and ecological planning and zoning for state and municipal level staff.
3.23   Conserving Biodiversity. This sub-component would increase the efficiency and
effectiveness of protection of the country's priority ecosystems and endangered and endemic
species. To achieve this objective, the sub-component would support two programs: (i) the
protected areas program, and (ii) wildlife protection and conservation program.
3.24 PROTECTED AREAS PROGRAM (US$38.6 million). The protected areas program
would finance activities aimed at protecting up to 17 protected areas around the country Oisted in
Annex 1.5). The government selected these 17 areas following a two-step process. First,
SEDUE identifled those areas that are of greatest global importance in terms of the biodiversity
and endemic species they protect. Within this group, SEDUE then selected areas which do not
have access to other sources of outside funds or which have the potential (through tourism or
other activities for which the protected areas could charge fees) to recover a significant portion of
the costs of protection.
3.25   Under the protected areas program, GET and counterpart funds would finance: (i)
consultant services to assist SEDUE in preparing a management plan for each of the 17 protected
areas (US$1.3 million); (ii) based on the results of the management plan, items contained in the
corresponding operating plan for each protected area, including: (a) basic infrastructure
investments (e.g. visitor administration headquarters, guard posts and houses, fire detection
towers, fences and nature trails) (approximately US$6.9 million for all 17 protected areas), (b)
equipment and vehicles (e.g. trucks, four-wheel drive vehicles, boats, power generators, and radio
and first aid equipment) (US$2.7 million), (c) demarcation of protected area borders and
consultant services (US$3.4 million), and (d) incremental operating expenditure for the protected
areas (e.g. incremental technical staff and rangers, materials, maintenance, etc.) (US$20.0
million); (iii) the costs of periodic meetings of local governmental and non-governmental groups
(TACs - see para. 3.76) to advise and assist the director of each protected area (US$0.5 million);
(iv) training programs for protected areas administrators and staff (US$0.4 million); (v)
preparation of an eco-tourism plan for one protected area (para. 3.26, below) (US$0.2 million);
(vi) participation of Mexican and non-Mexican specialists in reviewing management and operating
plans, as well as progress in program implementation (US$0.5 million); and (vii) program
coordination (IJS$0.6 million). These estimates are based on indicative activities in the protected
areas; each area's operating plan would provide a firm estimate of the costs to be incurred under
the program. During 1992, while management plans are being prepared for the 17 protected
areas, the program would provide a minimum level of support to carry out emergency protection



- 22 -
plans in 13 of the 17 areas (US$2.1 million).' Emergency plans would finance such items as
construction of entraTnce posts in some protected areas; the purchase of one or two vehicles and
radio, firefighting, and first aid equipment for each protected area; and the hiring of a director
and between 6 and 12 rangers for each area.
3.26   Several of the protected areas selected would also be appropriate for limited eco-tourism
development that could generate resources to cover a portion of the recurrent costs of
conservation activities. The component would not finance any eco-tourism investments, but
would contribute to the development of an eco-tourism strategy for the SINAP by funding the
preparation of an eco-tourism plan for Special Biosphere Reserve Isla Contoy in the state of
Quintana Roo in southern Mexico which would determine: (i) the potential market for eco-
tourism in the protected area; (i) the level of tourism that the protected area could support
without compromising its conservation objectives; (iii) the types and location of infrastructure that
would be appropriate to serve potential tourists; and (iv) the conditions under which SEDUE
should auction concessions to developers to establish and operate tourism facilities. The result of
this analysis would provide the basis for developing eco-tourism plans in other protected areas.
3.27   While the protected areas program would be targeted to 17 protected areas, the Bank
would take a final decision on the level and application of GET support for each area after the
management plan and the corresponding operating plan for that protected area have been
completed. The Bank would determine each area's eligibility for GET support on the basis of
criteria agreed by the government and the Bank. These criteria (contained in Annex 2) include
the existence of adequate land-use regulations, consistency of the objectives of the management
plan with GEF objectives, and the quality of the management plan (and corresponding operating
plan) for the protected area. At negotiations, agreement was confirmed on these criteria (para.
4.1 (a)). Considerable progress has already been made in preparing management plans for eight
of the 17 protected areas. In determining whether each protected area is in compliance with the
agreed eligibility criteria, the Bank would take into account the views of two technical specialists
selected from a group of thirteen Mexican and non-Mexican technical specialists who, after
reviewing the corresponding documentation and visiting the protected area, would submit
independent reports of their findings and recommendations to the government and the Bank. At
ngegotiations, agreement was confirmed regarding the initial composition and terms of reference of
the 13 Mexican and non-Mexican technical specialists, and assurances were received that the
government will not change the composition and terms of reference of this group without prior
agreement of the Bank (para. 4.1 (b)).
3.28   Once the Bank has determined that the protected area meets the basic eligibility criteria
for GET financing (per para. 3.27), the Bank would evaluate the corresponding operating plan to
determine the appropriate level of GET financing. The Bank would determine the level of GET
support to be allocated to each protected area on the basis of the operating plans and information
about other sources of financing currently available for the protected area. The operating plans
'The appraisal mission reviewed emergency plans for the following protected areas: (i)

(vi) PN El Chico; (vii) PN Izta-Popo; (viii) REB Mariposa Monarca; (ix) REB Isla Isabel; (x) PN
Lagunas de Chacahua; (xi) RB Sian Ka'an; (xii) REB Isla Contoy; (xiii) REB Ria Lagartos

would contain detailed budgets including both capital and recurrent cost expenditures over a four
year period. In determining the allocation of GET resources to be made to each protected area,
the Bank would seek to maximize the diversity of the challenges faced under the program, as well
as the approaches employed to address those challenges. At negotiati  , agreement was
confirmed regarding the process for preparing and reviewing management and operating plans,
and allocating GET resources to finance the operating plans (para. 4.1 (c)). Prior to
disbursements on each operating plan: (i) the protected area in question would be determined by
the Bank to be eligible for GET financing; (ii) the operating plan would be satisfactory to the
Bank; (iii) the corresponding TAC (see para. 3.76) would have been established under
arrangements acceptable to the Bank; and (iv) satisfactory contractual arrangements, as
appropriate, for the collaborative management of the protected area would be in place (para. 4.6).
3.29 WIDLIFE PROTECTION AND CONSERVATION PROGRAM (US$18.0 million).
The objective of this program would be to protect Mexico's wildlife from illegal poaching and
traffic. To achieve this objective, SEDUE/SE would strengthen its efforts in: (i) surveillance and
inspection of wildlife commerce,5 (ii) rehabilitation of confiscated wildlife, and (iii) protection of
nesting sites of endangered marine turtle species.
3.30   The program would increase SEDUE/SE's capability to enforce Mexican law regarding
the traffic and use of wild flora and fauna by supporting a network of seven regional centers
based in locations through which a high proportion of the wildlife trade flows. Each center would
be staffed by three mobile teams of inspectors who would carry out their "surveillance" function
by visiting key sites associated with wildlife commerce, such as pet stores, markets, veterinary
clinics, tanners, taxidermists, airports, truck stops and train stations. In carrying out their
"inspection" function, these inspectors would also respond to public complaints from citizens and
civic groups. Upon encountering violations of the wildlife law, the inspectors would initiate legal
procedures required for confiscation of the organisms and/or wildlife products and would provide
evidence and testimony to support the prosecution of offenders. At the same time, the mobile
units would transfer confiscated animals and plants to the nearest appropriate rehabilitation center,
whether operated directly by SEDUE/SE or by other concerned organizations or individuals.
SEDUE/SE would complement this enforcement function by producing and distributing
educational materials to educate the public on the regulations governing the use of wildlife and the
importance Mexico's wildlife, and by strengthening cooperation and coordination with other
relevant government entities.6
3.31   Consistent with the government's policy of decentralization and deconcentration of front-
line natural resource management functions, by the end of the third year of the project,
5"Surveillance" refers to those activities aimed at the prevention and detection of illegal traffic
in wildlife and related products. NIDspection" is the activity that allows public officials to
confiscate any equipment used in illegal wildlife traffic as well as any protected wildlife or related
products to be used for personal or commercial purposes. Inspection includes the legal and
administrative steps that may lead to the imposition of a fine or other sanction.
6Such as the Secretaria de Marina (SEMAR), SARH, the Secretaria de la Defensa Nacional
(SEDENA), the Procuradoria General de la Republica (PGR), and state and municipal
governments.



- 24 -
SEDUE/SE would transfer its 'surveillance" responsibility to state and municipal governments (in
coordination with non-governmental organizations). SEDUE/SE would retain for itself the
functions of coordination and supervision of front-line surveillance efforts and "inspection," which
would continue to be carried out by the regional units.
3.32   As a result of increased efforts to enforce legislation regulating wildlife commerce,
SEDUE/SE expects a dramatic increase in the amount of confiscated wildlife. In order to handle
this increase, the project would support the expansion and improvement of the national network of
wildlife rehabilitation centers. To the extent possible, the centers would re-introduce these
organisms to their natural habitats. When rehabilitation is not possible (due to injury or
domestication), the centers would relocate the animals and plants to zoos, research labs,
propagation centers or other facilities.7 The project would support seven regional centers,
corresponding to the seven mobile team bases. Five of the seven centers are already in operation;
four require minor expansion and remodelling. The project would support this expansion, as well
as the construction of two additional centers, and the operating costs of all seven centers. The
centers would provide veterinary care and, to the degree possible, would rehabilitate the skills
required for survival in the wild.
3.33   Finally, the wildlife protection program would support efforts to increase the population
levels of the seven species of marine turtles found in Mexico. This would be done through
effective management and patrolling of nesting sites, and through an educational campaign aiming
to inform the public of the threat that illegal harvesting poses to the long term survival of these
turtle species. The program would also support research activities to increase scientific
understanding of the biology and ecology of the turtles in order to develop data for the long term
management of the resource. Specifically, the program would support the construction and
operation of twelve camps, which would serve both as administrative centers for the program and
provide housing for the staff involved in turtle protection, research and environmental education.
During the breeding season, staff would patrol the nesting beaches to prevent the poaching of
turtles and eggs. Staff of the centers would carry out educational campaigns to educate local
communities about Mexican laws prohibiting the exploitation of marine turtles. The research
program would involve carrying out periodic censuses of turtle populations, studies of nesting
behavior and success, as well as general studies on turtle biology and ecology. As part of the
research effort, some of the turtles would be tagged in order to monitor population movements,
mortality rates and life expectancy. The project would provide specialized training to the staff in
the camps in order to enhance their skills and knowledge.
C.2   Modernization of Environmental Management Component
3.34   The specific objectives of this component would be: (i) tG strengthen the overall
management and administration of SEDUE/SE; (ii) to support the process of delegation of
responsibility and authority from SEDUE's central offices to its state delegations, and the process
of decentralization from SEDUE/SE to emerging environmental agencies in the states; and (iii) to
complement the governmnent's current command and control approach to environmental protection
71n the case of animals that have suffered severe injury or trauma and cannot be rehabilitated,
the centers would terminate the animal's life in a humane fashion.



- 25 -
and natural resources management with incentives-based economic instruments to achieve the
same objectives. The component would include three programs: (i) the institutional strengthening
program, (ii) the decentralization pilot program, and (iii) the economic incentives for the
environment program, and would be managed by SEDUE/SE's Coordinating Unit of Advisers to
the Under-Secretary - para. 3.71).
3.35  Institutional Strengthening Program (IS$4.5 million). The objective of the institutional
strengthening program would be to assist SEDUE/SE in increasing its managerial and
administrative efficiency in the short term and in further developing and implementing a strategy
to transform the agency into a second-tier institution. The program would include an institutional
analysis of SEDUE/SE (including its relation to SEDUE's state delegations) to be carried out by a
management consulting firm, and an action program to effect the changes recommended by the
consultants. The terms of reference for the consultants who would carry out the institutional
analysis were reviewed at appraisal and found to be satisfactory. SEDUE/SE has already
contracted the consultants and established an internal advisory group, whose composition is
satisfactory to the Bank, that will meet periodically with the consultants during the six-month first
phase of the institutional analysis, and coordinate implementation of the consultants'
recommendations during the second phase of the consultants' work. The Coordinating Unit of
Advisers to the Under-Secretary of SEDUE/SE will provide the leadership for this advisory
group, which will also include representation from each of SEDUE/SE's four general directorates,
and SEDUE's Coordinating Unit of State Delegates and General Directorate for Legal Affairs
(DGAJ).
3.36   The consultants would carry out the institutional analysis over a six month period, ending
June 30, 1992. Intermediate products would be: (i) a diagnosis of the current functioning of
SEDUE/SE (including an analysis of the degree to which the sub-secretariat's work program and
structure are consistent with its objectives; the relationship between SEDUE/SE, SEDUE's state
delegations, and state and municipal governments; and internal evaluation processes and
procedures); (ii) a medium-term plan defining how SEDUE/SE would function as a second-tier
institution and the steps necessary to carry out the plan; (iii) a proposed organizational structure
consistent with the medium-term plan; (iv) a design for a system by which SEDUE/SE can
continually evaluate its effectiveness; and (v) a design of an information system for the sub-
secretariat (including a computer network and accessories, software and communications
equipment). The consultants would pay particular attention to assessing the human resources
qualifications and infrastructure support needed to increase the efficiency of SEDUE/SE's
inspection and surveillance functions. At negotiations, agreement was reached that: (i) by July
31, 1992, the government will submit to the Bank for its recommendations and comment the
reports of the consultants carrying out the institutional analysis of SEDUE (para. 4.2 (f)); and (ii)
that on the basis of the consultants' reports and recommendations, and taking into account the
Bank's recommendations and comments, the Government would reorganize SEDUE according to
an action program (including staffing and budgetting targets to carry out SEDUE's mandate)
acceptable to the Bank to be prepared by December 3i, 1992 (para. 4.2 (f)). After completing
the first phase of the institutional analysis, it is expected that the consultants would continue to
provide support to SEDUE/SE in carrying out their recommendations and in providing staff
training.
3.37   Decentralization Pilot Program (US$5.6 million). The objective of the decentralization
pilot program would be to test in a few states how front-line pollution control and natural



- 26 -
resources management functions could be decentralized, reserving second-tier oversight functions
to SEDUE/SE. The program would support pilot activities to decentralize functions from
SEDUE/SE to up to five state environmental agencies in accordance with the philosophy of the
General Ecology Law. A small coordinating group for the program would be established within
the Coordinating Unit of Advisors to the Under-Secretary of SEDUE/SE to work closely with
participating states to develop and implement an investment plan that would support the states'
capacity to carry out front-line functions in pollution control and natural resources management.
These investment plans could include technical assistance to refine the organizational structure for
the state agencies, acquisition of information and communications technology, development of
training and administrative manuals, staff training, and preparation and implementation of a few
substantive programs identified as high priority by each state. The coordinating group would
evaluate the progress in the effective transfer of front line functions to the participating states.
Dibursements on investment programs in each state would be conditioned on: (i) agreement
between SEDUE/SE and the Bank on the detailed action plan for that state; and (ii) an operating
agreement signed by the SEDUE/SE and the state government, stating the respective roles and
responsibilities of each party, including the mechanisms by which SEDUE/SE would procure and
transfer to the state all civil works, goods and consultant services described in the agreed action
plan, and the state's commitment to provide its own resources to cover all recurrent costs
involved (para. 4.5 (b)).
3.38   SEDUE/SE has already selected two states to participate in the pilot program in the first
year - Jalisco and Tamaulipas. The criteria used to select these states were: (i) the Governor's
demonstrated commitment to address environmental issues; (ii) institutional readiness to accept
greater responsibility for pollution control and natural resources management; (iii) prospects for
continuity within the state administration for at least two years; (iv) availability in the state of
technical support (for example, from universities); (v) closeness of the relations between the state
agency and SEDUE's state delegation; and (vi) possibilities for complementarity with other
projects financed by the Bank. Based on the experience gained in these two states, the
coordinating group (para. 3.37) could work with one additional state in the second year of the
project, and two additional states in the third year. At negotiations, agreement was reached that,
on the basis of the above selection criteria, one additional state could be selected by the
Government and the Bank at the first annual evaluation meeting, and up to two more at the
second annual evaluation meeting (para. 4.2 (g)).
3.39   Economic Instruments for the Environment (US$1.2 million). The objective of this
program would be to help prepare a policy framework for the use of economic instruments that
could complement current command and control measures to achieve environmental objectives.
The government has formed a special inter-secretarial task force (Task Force on Modernization of
Environmental Management) comprised of SEDUE and the Secretaria de Hacienda y Credito
Puflico (SHCP) to oversee the implementation of a number of studies and to prepare a final
report with policy recommendations and an action program based upon the results of the studies.
The task force would serve as the locus for policy dialogue between the Bank and the government
on economics and environmental policy.
3.40  Initial studies to be commissioned in this program would seek to answer two basic
questions: How should the federal government ensure (i) a sustainable flow of resources to
environmental management functions? and (ii) consistency between economic and environmental
policies, and how could the government employ economic and other policy instruments to achieve



- 27 -
environmental policy objectives?
3.41   In order to address these questions, the program would support four sets of studies in the
area of environmental and natural resource economics as follows: (i) sources and uses of funds
by the environmental sector at the federal, state and municipal levels; (ii) sources and uses of
funds for environmental protection by federal agencles outside of the environmental sector; (iii)
expenditures on environmental protection by the private sector; and (iv) analysis of the
enviromnental impact of selected economic policies. Annex 2.3 provides the terms of reference
of these studies, which are satisfactory to the Bank.
3.42   At negotiations, agreement was reached: (i) on the structure and composition of the task
force and its responsibilities; (ii) on the mechanism to agree on the terms of reference of any
research and policy work to be carried out in subsequent years; (iii) that the government will
submit tu the Bank by June 30, 1993 the reports of the consultants carrying out these studies and
allow the Bank ene month to comment on the reports and on the policy recommendations and
proposed plans of action prepared by the inter-secretarial task force; and (iv) that the inter-
secretarial task force on modernization of environmental management will issue by November 30,
1993 its report on the results of the studies on economic instruments and environmental
management and its policy recommendations and proposed plans of action (para. 4.2 (h)).
C.3   Legislation and Standards Component (US$4.4 million)
3.43   Legislation. SEDUE's DGAJ would carry out two sets of studies with the objective of
preparing draft legislation for the consideration of the Mexican legislature. Upon completion of
the studies, the results would be discussed with the Bank and agreements would be reached on
follow-up actions as necessary (Annex 3.2).
3.44   The first set of studies (expected to be carried out in the first year of the project) aims to
define environmental crimes. Four specific studies would: (i) compile existing legal dispositions
into a typology of infractions of the penal code; (ii) develop a classification of human behavior
that is damaging to the environment; (iii) assess social demand for protection of the environment
through application of the penal code; and (iv) evaluate penalties that could be imposed on
violators of existing legislation that protects the environment. The result of this set of specific
studies would be draft legislation to incorporate crimes against the environment into the Mexican
penal code.
3.45   The second set of studies (expected to be carried out in the second year of the project)
would evaluate possible conflicts between the objectives of the General Ecology Law and other
Mexican legislation, with special emphasis on: (i) Water Law; (ii) Forest Code; (iii) Fishing
Law; and (iv) Agrarian Reform Law. In carrying out these studies, the consultants would be
required to interview government officials involved in various aspects of the above-mentioned
laws (including those involved in front-line enforcement) in order to detect possible conflicts in
interpretation of standing legislation by those charged with enforcing it. If the consultants identify
conflicts among standing legislation, they will draft legislation to be submitted to the Mexican
legislature to resolve such conflicts.
3.46   Regulation and Standards. SEDUE/SE has developed an ambitious program to prepare



- 28 -
and issue regulations and technical standards for the General Ecology Law, Including: (i) a
regulation on environmental risk; (ii) 87 additional technical standards in the areas of water and
air poliution control, management and disposal of hazardous wastes, and environmental risk
management; and (iii) 17 technical guidelines for preparing sectoral environmental impact
assessments. At negotations, agreement was reached on the timetable for issuing the standards to
be prepared under the project (para. 4.2 (i) and Annex 2. 1). In order to expedite the process of
issuing a large number of technical standards in a short time period, the DGNRE would contract
consulting firms to carry out the basic studies required prior to drafting technical standards. In
addition, the DGNRE would contract a consulting firm to prepare terms of reference and to
supervise preparation of technical documents and draft proposed technical standard, regulation or
technical instruction. In order to strengthen SEDUE/SE's staff in the area of environmental
standards, SEDUE/SE would hold a seminar in early 1992 for SEDUE/SE staff on comparative
experience in preparing technical standards, send staff to brief training courses in other countries,
and subscribe to technical journals related to environmental protection.
3.47   With regard to regulating environmental risk from hazardous substances, SEDUE would
prepare and issue a single technical standard for a large group of hazardous substances and
industrial processes, depending on the characteristics of each substance (toxicity, flammability,
explosiveness, and corrosiveness). SEDUE would also issue a standard on transporting hazardous
substances, and a regulation on risk management in industrial processes (Annex 2.1).
C.4   Stdes Component
3.48   The project would finance four sets of studies which would contribute to the preparation
of project proposals for investments under the second phase of the government's environmental
program.
3.49   Consolidatign of SINAP (US$1.1 million). The primary objectives of this set of studies
would be: (i) to identify gaps in the protection of ecosystems and species in the present SINAP;
(ii) to develop proposals for the incorporation of key areas into the SINAP to fill these gaps; (iii)
to identify the needs of the protected areas in terms of infrastructure, equipment and human
resources; (iv) to propose and prepare terms of reference for studies to broaden the knowledge
base of each protected area so as to improve its management; and (v) to analyze and describe the
legal framework governing the use and management of protected areas.
3.50 To achieve these objectives, SEDUE/SE's DGCERN would identify key ecosystems
which, due to their ecological uniqueness, high level of endemism or lack of protection
elsewhere, should be incorporated into the SINAP. Based upon this initial analysis, the
DGCERN would contract consulting firms or universities to draft proposals for incorporating
these areas into the SINAP. The studies regarding the needs of the existing 66 protected areas
would be carried out under DGCERN's leadership by six multidisciplinary teams, each
responsible for evaluating the protected areas within their given region. The DGCERN would also
contract out the study of the laws and regulations which govern the use and management of
protected areas. Terms of reference for these studies were reviewed and found satisfactory at
appraisal.
3.51   The expected products of these studies would be: (i) a report identifying gaps in



- 29 -
ecosystem and species protection in Mexico; (ii) a report recommnending the incorporation of each
new area into the SINAP; (iii) a report describing the findings of the evaluation of the 66
protected areas In terms of infrastructure, equipment, human resources and information needs;
(iv) terms of reference for studies to broaden the knowledge base for the management of the
protected areas; and (v) a report on the legal framework goveming the use and management of
Mexico's protected areas. Upon completion of these studies, the results would be discussed with
the Bank and agreements would be reached on follow-up actions as necessary (Annex 3.2).
3.52   Creation of Biological Corridors (US$0.4 million). The primary objective of this set of
studies would be to develop a strategy to enhance the ecological viability of individual protected
areas and migratory routes by the creation of biological corridors that would increase the effective
range area for wildlife currently found in protected areas.
3.53   In order to achieve this objective, the SEDUE/SE's DGCERN would first identify
potential areas for the creation of biological corridors linking key protected areas and providing
protection for wildlife migratory routes. At the same time, the DGCERN would also determine
information gaps and prepare terms of reference for specific studies to be carried out by
consulting firms or universities to assess the technical and socio-economic feasibility of given
corridors. Following completion of the studies, it is expected that SEDUE would draft and issue
decrees establishing the corresponding corridors.
3.54   The expected products of these studies would be: (i) a report documenting and
recommendign the priority areas for the establishment of biological corridors; (ii) specific studies
justifying the creation and management of each proposed corridor; and (iii) draft legal decrees
establishing the corridors. Upon completion of these studies, the results would be discussed with
the Bank and agreements would be reached on follow-up actions as necessary (Annex 3.2).
3.55   Environmental Effecs on Health (US$0.3 million). The primary objectives of this study
would be: (i) to identify potential health risks to populations exposed through work or domicile
location to toxic substances managed or emitted by industries; (ii) to develop and test training
activities aimed at medical personnel and the community-at-large; and (iii) to coordinate data
collection and analysis between the Secretariat of Health and SEDUE/SE such that sources of
exposure to toxic substances are identified and dealt with rapidly and adequately.
3.56   The study would be carried out in the city of Tijuana, Baja California during the first year
of the project. The Direccion General de Salud Ambiental (DGSA/SS) of the Secretaria de Salud
(SS) selected Tijuana on the basis of the following criteria: (i) representativeness of the
distribution of industries managing toxic substances; (ii) existence of adequate health services
infrastructure; (iii) prior involvement with the DGSA/SS in training courses for health inspectors;
(iv) interest of local community and health authorities in participating in the study; and (v)
accessibility of firmns. After completion of the study, the DGSA/SA plans to employ the
methodologies, curricula and materials developed in Tijuana in other industrialized urban areas.
3.57   The study would consist of the following specific activities:
o    Collection and analysis of information on industries in the Tijuana area that manage toxic
substances. The consultants would collect data from SEDUE/SE's inventory of firms,
complemented with information from the Instituto Nacional de Estadisticas. Geografia y



- 30-
lnformacion (NEGI), the SS, and other studies of toxic substances in Tijuana (including
studies carried out by the Colegio de la Frontera Norte, and efforts sponsored by UCLA
In Los Angeles, California USA).
o    Development of an information system that facilitates the collection, retrieval and up-
dating of information on the presence and management of industries dealing with high
volumes of toxic substances in Tijuana.
o    Based on priorities identified in the data collection and analysis stage, and actual
knowledge of health effects of common industrial toxic substances, determination of
training and information needs for medical personnel, health inspectors, and the
community-at-large.
o    Development and testing of training courses and materials for medical personnel, health
inspectors and the community-at-large.
3.58   As a result of the study: (i) Tijuana would have a health needs assessment with regard to
exposure to industrial toxic substances; (ii) local health services and environmental authorities
would have an information system and data base on high risk industries located in Tijuana; (iii)
about 100 local medical personnel, 30 local and federal health inspectors, and 5 federal
environmental health coordinators would receive training; (iv) the local community would be
educated about the means of avoiding or decreasing health risks from indoor and outdoor
environmental pollution and exposure to toxic chemicals; and (v) the DGSA/SS would gain
experience, methodologies and materials with which to replicate the study in other industrial
urban areas around the country. Upon completion of this study, the results would be discussed
with the Bank and agreements would be reached on follow-up actions as necessary (Annex 3.2).
3.59 E_nvironment Futures Study (US$0.3 million). The objective of this study would be to
construct a model relating economic, cultural and social activities in Mexico with the quality of
various elements of the Mexican environment (e.g., forests, wildlife, water, and air). Such a
model can then be used by the government in its long-term planning and decision-making
processes.
3.60  The study would consist of: (i) a review of the literature and selection of critical
environmental quality indicators; (ii) building "future' scenarios of activities critical to the
Mexican environment; (iii) an analysis of the influence of these events on the Mexican
environment at intervals of 10, 20 and 30 years; (iv) definition of a mechanism that will allow the
government to update the future scenarios as necessary and to identify data and information needs
and gaps; and (v) communication of study results to Mexican decision-makers and to the
interested public. The project would include one or more seminars/retreats with decision-makers
and opinion leaders in Mexico to communicate and discuss the study's conclusions and
recommendations. In addition, SEDUE/SE would publish a fact sheet at the conclusion of the
study addressed to the interested public describing the conclusions and the recommendations of
the study. Upon completion of this study, the results would be discussed with the Bank and
agreements would be reached on follow-up actions as necessary (Annex 3.2).

D.2   Financing Plan
3.65   The Bank loan of US$50 million equivalent would finance 44 percent of the estimated
costs of the project, net of taxes, including 100 percent of estimated foreign exchange costs and
33 percent of local costs. Local cost financing under the project is justified by the need to ensure
a significant impact on the country's institutional and policy framework for natural resources
management and environmental protection, and by the project's low foreign exchange costs. The
GET grant of US$30 million equivalent would finance 78 percent of the costs of the protected
areas program, including 100 percent of investment costs and 60 percent of the program's
recurrent costs. The Mexican government would provide counterpart funds of US$46.6 million
equivalent to cover the remaining project costs, including the cost of all taxes and all permanent
staff (except for staff hired under the project to work in SINAP protected areas funded by the
GET grant). Prior to loan effectiveness: (i) the government would demonstrate that adequate
funds have been allocated in the federal budget for project implementation in FY92 (January 1,
1992 to December 31, 1992); and (ii) BANOBRAS (as financial agent) would enter into
contractual arrangements, satisfactory to the Bank, providing for the transfer to the government of
the proceeds of the loan and the GET grant for eligible expenditures (para. 4.3). Prior to GET
grant effectiveness, the Loan Agree,ment for the proposed Bank loan to BANOBRAS would
become effective (para. 4.4). Table 3.2 presents the project financing plan.

review the level of GET resources already committed through approved operating plans

satisfactory management and operating plans are completed for other additional protected areas.
3.67   Rg-ffent L oMa1. The 1990 budget of SEDUE's sub-secretariat for Ecology represented
less than 10 percent of SEDUE's budget or only 0.06 percent of the total federal budget. The
Sub-secretauiat's 1991 budget, including a large allocation to cover expected project costs in 1991,
is almost three times its 1990 level as a proportion of the total federal budget (representing 23
percent of SEDUE's budget and 0.17 percent of the total federal budget).
3.68   Over the four-year project period, the number of persons working as fixed-term or
permanent staff in SEDUE/SE will rise and then fall to about the same number as currently work
in the Sub-secretariat. The cost of these individuals, however, will increase substantially as lower
level staff are replaced by more higuly qualified and experienced staff, consistent with
SEDUE/SE's transformation into a second tier institution. By the fourth year of the project, the
recurrent costs of the project will be about 30 percent higher than in the first year of the project.
Incremental recurrent costs of the project would be financed from the loan on a declining basis
Crable 3.4: Allocation of Loan Proceeds), and no proceeds from the loan would be used to
finance salaries of permanent staff of SEDUE. In light of the tiny fraction of the federal budget
that is represented by SEDUE/SE's capital and recurrent budgets, and a project design that phases
in counterpart absorption of recurrent costs over the duration of the project, no difficulty in
government absorption of increased costs of a strengthened SEDUE/SE is expected.
3.69   The protected areas program is expected to result in an incremental recurrent cost burden
of approximately US$6.8 million annually, by the end of the project period. By that time, the
government expects to be able to cover about 75 percent of the recurrent costs of the program
from receipts in the protected areas. As with the rest of the proposed Environmental Project,
incremental recurrent costs would be financed from the GET grant on a declining basis (Table
3.S: Allocation of GET Grant Proceeds). At negotiations, agreement was confirmed regarding
the timetable for phasing out GET financing of the program's recurrent costs (para. 4.1 (e)).
'For example, if US$12 million of GET financing is approved by September 30, 1992, then
the amount to be canceled from the grant would be:

E.    Project Qrganization and Management
E. 1   Project Management
3.70   Since a principal objective of the project is to support SEDUE/SE in increasing its
effectiveness in carrying out key environmental protection and natural resources management
functions that the agency already carries out, there is no need to create additional project
impiemezuation srucwures within the agency. Direct responsibility for implementing most of the
proposed programs would lie with the General Directorates of SEDUE/SE, in coordination with
corresponding personnel located in SEDUE's delegations in the states.
3.71   The DGPCCA would be responsible for executing the pollution control sub-component;
the DGNRE, the environmental impacts sub-component and the studies on regulations and
standards; the DGCERN, the biodiversity sub-component and the studies on consolidating SINAP
and on biological corridors. With regard to the studies, the DGCERN would not carry out the
work itself, but would contract with research institutions or non-governmental organizations with
the required expertise to conduct the studies. The Coordinating Unit of Advisers to the Under-
Secretary of SEDUE/SE (assisted by its small coordinating group -- para. 3.37) would be
responsible for executing the institutional strengthening program and the decentralization pilot
program (in coordination with the five states involved). This Unit would also provide the
secretariat for the Task Force on Modernization of Environmental Management. SEDUE's
General Directorate for Legal Affairs (DGAJ) would be responsible for implementing the legal
studies. The Secretariat for Health would participate in implementing the pre-investment study on
environmental impacts on health. CONADE would participate in the environmental futures study.
3.72   PMoie(ct Coordination Unit (PCUI. There is already a unit within CONADE which,
flnanced in part through the end of 1991 by a small grant from UNDP, took the lead in
preparing the proposed project. While existing units within SEDUE/SE and outside of SEDUE
would be directly responsible for implementing specific programs (as described in para. 3.71), the
small PCU within CONADE would coordinate these efforts, maintain project accounts, carry out
monitoring and evaluation activities, prepare periodic reports on the project and prepare the
Project Completion Report. The PCU has already been staffed by SEDUE/SE with individuals
with experience and qualitications satisfactory to the Bank, and the PCU would report directly to
the Under-Secretary of SEDUE/SE. Atl,g ioQtiins, the Government of Mexico provided
assurances that the PCU would be maintained and continue to be staffed with individuals with
experience and qualifications satisfactory to the Bank (para. 4.2 (j)). Tle Director of the PCU
participated in the negotiations.
3.73  In addition to its role in coordinating implementation of the several programs financed
under the proposed project, the PCU would coordinate preparation of the second phase of the
Government's environmental program. In this second role, the PCU would build on the
proposed project and, consistent with the goal of SEDUE/SE to decentralize routine monitoring
and control functions, identify additional priority investments to be included in the Government's
proposed sectoral program.
3.74  Monitoring and evaluation. The PCU would be responsible for mnonitoring
implementation of all project components. The unit would carry out this task on the basis of the
output targets describod in Annexes 1.8, 2.1, 3.1 and 3.2, and quarterly evaluation meetings (in



- 35 -
February, May, August and November of each year). Regarding these quarterly meetings, at
negotiations, agreement was reached that:
(i)   two weeks before each meeting, SEDUE/SE will send the Bank the draft agenda in order
to provide the Bank the opportunity to suggest additional agenda items;
(ii)  following each meeting, SEDUE/SE will send the Bank the minutes of the meeting;
(iii) the minutes to the August quarterly meeting will include an estimate of the budgetary
allocation needed to i arry out the project the following year;
(iv)  the minutes of the November meeting (to be submitted to the Bank by December 1) will
include: (a) a report on the budget allocation request presented to the national legislature
for the upcoming calendar year, and (b) a detailed report describing the results of project
monitoring for the immediately prior 12 months, based on the implementation targets and
goals agreed at appraisal and detailed in Annexes 1.8, 2.1, 3.1 and 3.2, and signed by the
government and the Bank in a supplemental letter to the Guarantee Agreement, and
suggestions for improving project implementation and effectiveness;
(v)   the February meeting will be designated an annual review meeting and, in addition to the
usual participants from the implementing units, will be attended by representatives of
BANOBRAS and the Bank. The annual review will be based on the results of project
monitoring contained in the minutes of the November quarterly meeting, and will
conclude with agreement on an annual plan of action, including specific goals and
projections for project implementation for the calendar year during which the annual
review is held. The minutes for the February meeting will also include a report on the
budget allocation actually approved by the national legislature for the current fiscal year
(paras. 4.2 (k) and 4.2 (1)).
3.75   The annual review meeting will provide an opportunity for the government and the Bank
to discuss, among other things: (a) attainment of the objectives specified in the Strategy Letter
(Annex 1.6); (b) progress in carrying out the action plan for strengening and, if necessary,
restructuring SEDUE; (c) progress in meeting the schedules and targets established in the
Supplemental Letter to the Guarantee Agreement, signed by representatives of the government and
the Bank; (d) the design of the environmental auditing program; (e) progress in implementing the
recommendatioir, :tudies carried out under the project; (f) possible follow-up studies regarding
economic instruments for environmental protection and natural resource management; (g)
selection of states to participate in the pilot decentralization program; and (h) the adequacy of the
budgetary allocation obtained for implementing the project in the upcoming calendar year.
3.76 With regard to the protected areas program, SEDUE would create a technical advisory
council (TAC) in each of the 12 states where the protected areas are located. Each TAC would
include representatives from local communities, scientific groups, local and state government,
SEDUE's state delegation, and the private sector. Once each year, each TAC would meet in an
annual evaluation meeting with officials from SEDUE's headquarters in Mexico City and with the
external specialists who reviewed the investment plans for the protected areas corresponding to
that TAC. The external specialists would also visit the protected areas at this time, compare the
achievements of the program with the outplut indicators estimated at appraisal, and prepare reports



- 36 -
of their findings to send to SEDUE and the Bank. These reports would be discussed at the annual
review meetings (para. 3.74 (v)). At negotiations, agreement was confirmed on the guidelines for
the establishment and functioning of the TACs (para. 4.1 (d)).

by Bank supervision missions. BANOBRAS would provide SEDUE/SE advice and assistance in

3.78  Civil wrks. Civil works would represent about 8 percent of total project costs and
would be small in size. All civil works would be procured under LCB or local shopping
procedures, since no civil works contract would exceed US$500,000; up to US$8.9 million would
be procured following LCB procedures. Civil works costing less than US$150,000, up to an
aggregate amount equivalent to US$1.5 million may be procured following local shopping
procedures based on price quotations from at least three contractors.
3.79  Go. Computers, vehicles and goods would represent about 16 percent of total project
costs. To the extent possible, contracts for goods would be grouped to allow procurement of
larger packages. Packages exceeding US$250,000 would be procured following ICB procedures.
Six such ICB procurement packages (totalling US$9.3 million) are expected for: (i) computers
and accessories (US$0.6 million equivalent); (ii) vehicles (US$1.9 million); (iii) field equipment
for air quality monitoring (US$1.7 million); (iv) laboratory equipment (US$2.0 million); (v)
furniture (US$1.8 million); and (vi) radio and photographic equipment (US$1.3 million). Local
manufacturers would be granted a margin of preference in accordance with the Bank's Guidelines
for ICB procurement of goods. Packages exceeding US$50,000 but costing less than US$250,000
would be procured under LCB procedures; up to US$14.2 million (including US$8.0 million of
goods classified as incremental operating expenses) would be procured following LCB procedures.
Packages costing less than US$50,000, up to an aggregate amount equivalent to US$5.1 million
(including US$1.3 million of goods classified as incremental operating expenses), would be
procured under local shopping procedures based on price quotations from at least three suppliers.
3.80   Consultants. SEDUE/SE will follow the August 1981 Bank Guidelines for the Use of
Consultants by World Bank Borrowers and by the Bank as Executing Agency for the selection and
appointment of individual short-term and long-term consultants and consulting firms.
SEDUE/SE's standard contract for individual short-term and long-term consultants was reviewed
at appraisal and found acceptable. Individual consultant contracts (including long-term
consultants) would amount to an estimated US$21.7 million. Contracts with consulting firms
would amount to approximately US$25.0 million. All studies to be financed under the project
would be subject to terms of reference satisfactory to the Bank.
E.3   Disbursement
3.81   The proposed Bank loan and GET grant would be fully disbursed in four years; the
closing date would be December 31, 1995. Annex 4.4 contains the estimated disbursement
schedule of the Bank loan; Annex 4.5 contains the disbursement schedule of the GET grant. To
ensure timely and efficient project implementation, various start-up activities are already
underway. Retroactive financing from the loan would be available to finance up to US$5 million
(10 percent of the proposed loan) for eligible expenditures incurred in accordance with Bank
procurement guidelines after March 1, 1991 (para. 4.7). At negotiation$, agreement was reached
that claims for retroactive financing of eligible expenditures would be made no later than four
months after loan effectiveness (para. 4.2 (m)).

3.84   All disbursements related to contracts up to US$500,000 for works and US$250,000 for
goods would be made on the basis of certified statements of expenditures (SOEs) prepared by
BANOBRAS. BANOBRAS would retain documentation to support SOEs and make these
documents available to Bank supervision missions.
E.4   Accounts and Audits
3.85  To facilitate disbursement from the Bank loan and the GET grant, two special accounts
would be opened after loan and grant effectiveness in the Central Bank. The Bank would make
an initial deposit of US$5 million from the Bank loan into one account, and US$2.5 million from
the GET grant into the second account. The initial deposits would be sufficient to cover about
four months' estimated disbursements from the Bank loan and the GET grant, respectively. lhe
Bank would replenish these accounts as requested upon receipt of satisfactory evidence that
expenditures were eligible for financing out of the loan or the grant, as the case may be, and on



-40-
the basis of withdrawal applications (Form 1903) submitted by the Central Bank. All currency
conversions will be made in accordance with Bank policy.
3.86 Each participating agency, including SEDUE/SE and BANOBRAS, would maintain
separate accounts for project expenditures. At negotiations, agreement was reached that the
Guarantor and the Borrower would have project records and accounts audited each fiscal year by
independent auditors acceptable to the Bank, and furnish to the Bank as soon as possible, but In
any case no later than six months after the end of each fiscal year, a certified copy of the agreed
audit reports (para 4.2 (n)). The Bank would review the auditors' terns of reference to ensure
that they include the audit of the special account and SOEs. The auditors' report should Include
an opinion (and comments, as necessary) on the methods employed in compiling the statements of
expenditures; their accuracy; the relevance of supporting documents; their eligibility for financing
in terms of the project's legal agreement; and the standards of record-keeping and internal
controls related to the foregoing.
E.6 Project Supervision
3.87  Bank supervision of project execution will be particularly important and intensive during
the first two years of the project when significant institutional changes will be occurring. For this
reason, at least three Bank supervision missions each year would be needed. Staffing for these
missions would include a sector economist as well as specialists in institutional development,
pollution control, environmental laboratories, environmental impact assessment and biodiversity
conservation as necessary. Each year, SEDUE/SE and the Bank would conduct a systematic
annual reviews of progress in achieving the goals of the project (paras. 3.74 and 3.75). The
annual reviews meetings would be both retrospective and prospective.
3.88  Within the Bank, supervision will be coordinated with other related operations already
under implementation, such as the Water Supply and Sanitation Sector Project (Ln. 3271-ME) and
the Decentralization and Regional Development Project (Ln. 3310-ME), as well as with
preparation of a possible follow-up operation to the proposed project. Given SEDUE/SE's
extensive involvement in many Bank-financed and other projects, and its likely increased role
once agreement has been reached on a Mexico-USA-Canada free trade agreement, special
attention would be given to avoiding institutional overload.
F.    Benefits and Rislks
3.89   Benefits. Mexico's environmental problems are grave. The country can reap significant
benefits from investment in consolidating the institutional and policy framework for systematically
addressing these problems. The key functions component would result in immediate tangible
benefits in terms of monitoring and enforcing compliance with pollution standards, ensuring
adequate attention to environmental concerns while planning investment projects, controlling
wildlife trade and protecting Mexico's national system of protected areas. The project would
assist the government in strengthening SEDUE/SE to perform its leadership role in Mexico's
efforts to protect its environment.
3.90  Risks. By the time of the closing date (December 31, 1995), the proposed project is



- 41 -
expected to Increase the recurrent costs of SEDUE/SE by about 30 percent above the current
level. However, given that SEDUE/SE's 1991 budget accounts for less than 0.2 percent of the
total federal budget, this increase should be fully within the absorptive capacity of the federal
budget if the current strong political commitment holds. Two potential risks are that: (i) a
decline in apparent strong current political commitment to strengthen SEDUE/SE would lead to
the problems of inaction, failure to provide adequate counterpart funds in a timely manner, and a
slow pace of disbursement; and (ii) if some cost recovery or other mechanisms for ensuring a
sustainable resource base for SEDUE/SE are not achieved by the close of the proposed project,
SEDUE/SE's ability to sustain increased recurrent costs would be subject to the same budgetary
uncertainty that led to the weakening of the agency in the past. Project design minimizes these
risks by supporting the creation of an intor-secretarial task force to address the issues of sector
financing and the use of economic instruments, and by including in the project provisions for
monitoring the adequacy of the budget. An additional risk, regarding possible inability to
disburse the GET grant for the protected areas program, would be mitigated through the
procedure discussed in para. 3.66.

SummarX of the General Ecoloay Law
The General Law of Ecological Equilibrium and Environmental Protection:' 2
The General Ecology Law (GEL) which took effect in March 1988 and which
superseded earlier environmental statutes, regulates the protection,
restoration, and maintenance of ecological balance in Mexico. It is a
comprehensive document containing rules which define land-use management,
restoration and improvement of the environment, protection of natural areas
and plant and animal life, sound exploitation of natural resources, and
pollution prevention and control. The GEL places the primary authority for
enforcement of environmental laws, regulations, and standards with SEDUE3.
The Law is composed of the following titles:
Title I - General Arranqement:
The first three chapters of the Law establish preliminary norms governing
environmental protection in Mexico. Chapter 1 defines various ecological and
developmental terms and norms which used as components of the GEL. Chapter 2
describes the basis for resolving potential conflicts with concurrent state
and municipal environmentai statutes, and gives guidelines for the application
of the Law. The numerous components of the Law are also described in general
terms. In Chapter 3 the diverse responsibilities of SEDUE are outlined,
including those which apply uniquely to the Federal District. The degree of
coordination with the Federal Public Administrationi, and the jurisdictional
limits of SEDUE are established in this Section.
Chapters 4 and 5 of Title I describe the principles and political framework
governing Mexico's ecology, as well as the instruments used by SEDUE to
implement the GEL. Chapter 5 contains the following 9 sections which lay out
the political framework of the GEL:
I - Ecological planning
2 - Ecological regulations
3 - Ecological criteria for the promotion of development
4 - Ecological regulations and human settlement
5 - Evaluation of environmental impact
6 - Ecological technical norms
7 - Measures for the protection of natural areas
8 - Ecological investigation and education
9 - Information and enforcement
Of particular significance are Mexico's environmental impact regulations,
which are described in Section 5. These provide that, prior to construction,
all public or private works potentially harmful to the environment will be
subject to an assessment of the negative impacts they may cause, and this
'L2y General del Ecruilibrio Ecoloico v la Proteccion al Ambiente, 1988.
2AsDects of Mexico Envlronmental Policy and the Environmental Situation
in Mexigo. SEDUE, April 1991.
3Secretaria de Desarollo Urbano y Ecologia iSEDUE), or Secretariat of
Urban Development and Ecology. SEDUE has three sub-secretariats, one of which
is "Ecology."



- 46 -
Annex 1.1
page 2 of 4
assessment must suggest possible mitigation steps. SEDUE or another
appropriate federal agency may then impose conditions upon the proposed
activities which must be adhered to during implementation. To strengthen the
regulatory process, guidelines have been published which demonstrate how to
draw up environmental impact statements. All sectors producing goods and
services are required to submit environmental impact studies. In addition,
technical criteria have been formulated to evaluate environmentally sensitive
projects in the areas of aquaculture, forestry, construction of marinas and
highways, mining, and production and transmission of electric power. In the
case of particularly dangerous activities, a separate risk asseasment is also
required to mitigate the potential for environmental damage.
Title _I - Protected Areas:
Title II of the GEL provides information on Mexico's protected areas system
(El Sistema Nacional de Areas Naturales Protegidas or SINAP). SEDUE is
responsible for managing nine types of protected areas, each with a unique set
of management priorities, designed to achieve a sustainable balance &etween
the conservation of biodiversity and resource use and development. Protected
areas management is governed by both federal regulations and state and local
laws which outline the basic steps which must be taken in order to establish
and maintain a protected area.
Development within protected areas is regulated by federal, state, and
municipal laws, and must be approved by the responsible government agencies.
The Secretary of Agriculture and Water Resources for Agrarian Reform is
responsible for monitoring proposed development achemes, and providing
technical assistance to ejido4 communities wishing to develop natural
resources within a protected area.
National protected areas are created by a declaration of the federal
government in cooperation with the appropriate sectoral secretary, and must
contain the following components: a precisely demarcated area, a description
of allowable level of natural resource development, a management plan, and a
means of enforcing applicable regulations. These declarations describe how
the reserve will be managed in terms of administration, financing, community
involvement, and permissible type and level of development withLn the reserve.
The Law lists activities which are restricted within bioaphere reserve core
areas, as well as the most basic elements which should be included in a
reserve management plan.
Titl  II - Rational Development of Natural Resourcest
In this title, the GEL describes the basic criteria to be considered wlth
regard to the development of water, aquatic, and terreetrial resources. Thli
title also outlines the administrative structure for overseeing development,
rules for intersectoral cooperation in the development of natural resources,
and instruments for implementing and enforcing development regulations.
Included in the outline of land resource development, are those regulations
governing forestry and mining.
SEDUE maintains the right to revoke, modify, or suspend development
authorLzations whLch are duemed to greatly disrupt ecological equilibrlwu.
Particular attention is to be paid to the extraction of non-renewable
resources, and the potential associated environmental damage. The Law
establishes technical norms governing the protection of all natural resources
'Communally owned public land.



- 47 -
Xunnex 1.1
page 3 of 4
which may be. negatively affected by non-renewable resource extraction. Where
the restoration of degraded land takes place, and where the use of exotic
species is necessary for such restoration, the GEL states that the
introduction of compatible exotics will be a priority.
Title IV - Environmental Protection:
This title outlines SEDUE's strategy for the prevention and control of air,
water, and land pollution, as well as the means for mo.aitoring and enforcing
regulations. SEDUE, in cooperation with the Secretary of Health, is charged
with establishing and monitoring air quality standards, and with regulating
emissions levels which conform to human health tolerances, particularly in
urban areas such as the Federal District. State and municipal governments are
responsible for enforcing compliance with municipal and federal regulations
within their jurisdiction.
In determining land use and zoning for urban development, the GEL requires
SEDUE to consider topographic and climatic conditions with the aim of
facilitating the dispersion of atmospheric contaminants. In addition, the
appropriate governmental authorities are charged with enforcing the use by
industry, of the best economically available technology for the control of air
pollutants.
Title V - Social Participation:
This brief title establishes SEDUEs commitment to incorporating the views of
all affected groups into development decisions; these groups include non-
governmental organizations (NGOs), local communities, educational
institutions, and other societal representatives. This emphasis on greater
cooperative decision making, together with initiatives to more effectively
disseminate information and promote ecological education, comprisea an attempt
by SEDUE to increase public involvement in all aspects of policy making.
T-itle VI EnforECementt
The final title broadly describes the structure whereby SEDUE will monitor
conmpliance with the GEL, and enforce its regulatione. It includes sections on
inepection and enforcement, administrative sanctions, penalties for violation
of the law or its regulatione or technical norms, and popular complaints.

MEXICO
Environmental Proiect
Summary of Mexico's 1990-1994 National Environmental Protection PrograM' 2
Under the guidelines of the 1989-1994 National DevelopMent Plan, the
NAtional Envi onmental Protection Program outlines a national strategy aimed
at encouraging the rational use of natural resources while remedying past
environmental damage. By identifying the most important threats to Mexvco's
natural environment, and targeting the chief obstacles to environmental policy
reform, the Program seeks to transform the relationship between economic
development and environmental protection in Mexico. The Program addresses the
historically low priority given to environmental variables during much of
Mexico's development wii.h the intention of decreasing environmental
degradation and encouraging greater national sensitivity to such issues. Key
emphases of the program are described below:
Decentralization of AuthorLty. The Program is designed to allow states
and municipalities to initiate action through social groups interested
in protecting the environment. Environmental prefectures (regidurias
ecologicas) have been designed to facilitate joint programs at the grass
roots level. To date, 450 such prefectures have been established
following more than 200 courses offered by the Secretariat for Urban
Development and Ecology (SEDUE). As part of the decentralization plan,
the Program will support policies designed to facilitate intersectoral
communhcation and build consensus among communities.  International
cooperation will be sought to assist in the transfer of technical
knowledge and experience.
Ecol qical Planninu and Zoning.  The Program also calls for a
territorial strategy to address local and regional problems. SEDUE has
initiated a process of ecological planning and zoning that considers the
potential best use for each zone, the protection of ecosystems, and the
distribution of human communities and their eocio-economic activities.
Consistent with current urban policy, the Program includes policies to
induce the relocation of populations to stem concentration in the large
cities, and urban planning initiatives to ensure a balance between urban
development and the preservation of natural surroundings. Policies to
reduce automobile and industrial emissions will be included in this part
of the program.
!mxlementino the "Polluter Pavs PrinciPle".  The Program will pursue
policy initiatives which support the "polluter pays principle" as a
means of helping to eefray the cost of enforcing new emiesion
regulations and the clean-up of contaminated sites. The Federal
goversment is currently seeking to promote such initiatLves by making
state enterprises more accountable for environmental damaoe. All
sectors are expected to establish a framework to promote more accurate
representation of environmental costs in development decislons.
Interagencv Coordination. The Program uses an intersectoral strategy to
combine institutional efforts as a powerful tool for environmental

mnanagement. Policies are designed tc supplement public expenditure with
financial contributions from interested private sector groups and
lnstitutiono. This system is designed to convince enterprises producing
goods and services of the deuirability of installing anti-pollution
devices, thus avoiding expensive retrofitting and repair of
environmental damage. In coordinating Program actions, SEDUE has
bolstered the technical and administrative support for research
institutions by enlisting their participation in the process. In
additioh, the Program hae sought to increase communication with
ecological groups and non-governmenital organizations in Mexico.
The Program also outlines the principal threats to Mexico's natural
resources and protected areas eyetem (SINAP), and the measures which will be
taken to mitigate such threats including:
Natural Resources and Protected Areas:
a.   A program for the restoration of degraded ecosystems
b.   Expansibn of the protected areas system and consolidation of exijting
areae
c.   Establishment of new botanical and zoological gardens, and nurseries
d.   Establishment of units for: (i) ecological protection and restorationp
(ii) providing basic sanitation; and (iii) promoting greater
environmental awareness and community empowerment
Wate aPollutMion:
a.   Increased inspection and enforcement of pollution control standarde
b.   Eetatliahment of stricter norms for the discharge of waste into drains
and bodiea of water
c.   Expanoion of the National Water Quality Monitoring System
barj Polutioj:
a.   Support for the National Inventory of Air Pollution in high risk urban
areas, as well as a network for monitoring air quality throughout Mexico
b.   Promotion of energy conservation for vehicles and industry, including
installation of emiesion reduction devices in heavily polluted areas
C.   Negotiations with PEMEX and CPE to reach an Agreement on measuras to
reduce and recover environmental contaminants
2Di"oRa_Lof Solid Wastes:
a.   Regulation of industrial development near urban areas in order to locato
sites for the safe disponal of solid wastes
b.   Encourage Lndustrial practices whlich generate leos  solid waste, (l.e.
"clean technology")
c.   Initiate a new national inventory of industrial waste
a.   Formulation of environmental impact guidelines for  oach sector, with an
emphasis on those sectors which have the greatest potential to cause
environmental damago
b.   Consolidation of an Environmental Asnussment-RapLd Information System
c.   Application of Envlronmental Risk Assessment Procedures to all public or
private sector projects
d.   Creation of a guJide for the prevention and control of environmental
damage

Natural Protected Areas in-Mexico
natur,al--Protected Areas Management Categories
The second title (titulo) of the Ley General del ¢auilibrio Ecolooico v
l Proteccion al Anmbiente deocribes the Sistema Nacional de Areas Naturales
Eateaidas (SINAP) and defines categories of protected areas. The nine
categories of protected areas in Mexico and the total land area protected
under each category are described below.
A.    Areas of national interest:
1.   Biosuhere Reserves 14.323.8%9-W  - Protected areas of at least 10,000
hectares, that contain biogeographically relevant ecoaystems not greatly
altered by humans. They are distinguished by the distinction between
core areas which are to be strictly protected, and buffer zones, where
productive human activities would be allowed to take place on a
sustainable basis.
2.   gBeciol fsoshere Reserves (491100 Hal - Areas containing relatively
unaltered representative ecoeyotems with endemic, threatened or
endangered species. Their generally smaller area and ecosystem size
differentiate them from biosphere reserves.
3.   NW20IQn-LEaXkI66O. l69 j1a - Areas of outstanding scenic beauty, with
national or international significance for scientific, educational, and
recreational use. Areas may also be designated for historic value,
because of the existence of plants or animals of notLonal importance,
or because the area would be appropriate for tourism devolopment.
I'ra                   1,aXenj.2._ Aj_ 3    5,512,470 ZS
4.   fttuL, INonumetf - Relatively small areas created to strictly protect
nationally significant natural features because of their special
interest or unique cheracteriatics; such characteristics are usually
related to the historlc, scientific, or a9sthetic qualities of the
landscape. These areas must conform to the General Ecology Law and to
the Federal Forostry Law
S.   MUML           cparqk  - Marine areas or beaches with their contiguous
areas, dedicated to the preservation of aquatic ecosystems. The lower
specLfic norms of protection in these reserves allow a certain amount of
ocological lnvestigation and rational use of the resource. Theme areas
must conform to the Federal Fisheries Law, and the Federal  arine Law as
w*ll as all local regulations.
6.   N&tuIXjl[A I!2  5eD.-Arger - Areas dedicated to the preservation
and restoration of forest zones and the conservatlon of land and water.
These areas must conform with the General Ecology Law, and the Federal
Forestry and Water Laws. The following types of areas fall within this
broad category whivlh includes 59 arsea totaling approximately 8 million

MEXICO
Environmental Project
Translation of the Letter from the Government of Mexico Desgribing the
Strategy to TransfQrm SEDUE/SE into a Second-Tier Institution
Secretariat of Urban Development and Ecology
Subsecretariat of Ecology
Mexico City, 31 Jan. 1992
Mr. Rainer B. Steckhan,
Director
Country Department II
Latin America and the Caribbean
The World Bank
Washington, D.C.
As you are aware, the Subsecretariat of Ecology is trying to find the most suitable mechanisms for
increasing its capacity to prevent and control negative environmental impacts, as well as for preserving
the country's natural resources as effectively as possible.
Our ideas for the structure of a second-tier environmental institution, the principal objectives of this
institutional transformation, and the implications that we expect this to have are found in the enclosed
document.
In the hope that this document meets with the Bank's expectations, I remain,
Yours sincerely,
"SUFRAGIO EFECTIVO. NO REELECCION."
THE UNDERSECRETARY
SERGIO REYES LUJAN
cc.   Antonio Cervera Sandoval, Coordinator of Advisors for the Subsecretariat of International
Financial Affairs, S.H.C.P. [Secretariat of Finance and Public Credit].
