PROFILE OF A SECOND-TIER ENVIRONMENTAL INSTITUTION
BACKGROUND
The high priority that is accorded to environmental protection in Mexico is well-known; in fact,
more emphasis is being placed on questions of environmental protection today than ever before in the
country's history. This growing emphasis can be clearly measured in financial terms. In 1988, the
Subsecretariat of Ecology had a budget of USS 4.269 million for the execution of its programs; in 1991,
the corresponding budget was USS 38.869 million. This represents an annual growth rate of 109%,
which is unparalleled in any other sector. To this must be added expenditure by all the governmental
agencies as well as semi-public industry for this same work. In 1991, an amount of US$ 1.49 billion was
recorded as the Federation's budgetary outlay. This means that the public sector alone allocates close
to 0.7% of the GDP, which places Mexico alongside countries that devote a greater proportion of their
economic activity to investment in the name of achieving sustainable development.
When it comes to the running expenses of SEDUE, including money for staff salaries and
administrative support, the situation has not been as described above. In 1988, running expenses
amounted to around USS 4.7 million, but in 1991 they had only increased to US$ 5.2 million. In other
words, while activity grew by almost a factor of 10, the administrative structure remained practically
unchanged.
Tis structural background makes it easier to understand SEDUE's push toward insti.utional
strengthening. In spite of the economic and financial effort, the problems to be solved remain far-
reaching and complex. We need to take measures that reflect the high priority being placed on the
variable of environment today. As we are faced with the need for prevention and control in the current
age, it is indispensable to repair the damage that is the legacy of yesterday's neglect. At the same time
as the country is beginning to experience a renewal of growth, the will exists to pursue this path without
detriment to the development opportunities of our future generations.
In summary, expenditure is still insufficient, and the structure of enviromental management
needs to be strengthened. If we fail to address these two central issues, the country's capacity to control
pollution and ensure rational use of its natural resources will not keep pace with the modernization
process that is occurring in many sectors of our national life.
One of the strategies for dealing with financial difficulties which is outlined in the National
Environmental Protection Program is to follow a scheme in which "the polluter is the one who pays."
Until now, the management system has rested on the concepts of command and control, whose
possibilities have practically been exhausted. The Subsecretariat of Ecology has now decided to promote
a more effective and intensive mobilization of resources based on economic incentives, through applying
the principle that whoever causes damage should pay the cost of repairing it. If this approach is used,
only a slight Increase in the fiscal burden will give us the resources we need to create a better quality
environment.
In order to ensure that polPuters are charged an amount that reflects the amount of damage they
cause, it is essential not only to increase the capacity for determining whc is causing environmental
degradation, but also to expand the corresponding structure for measuring these ill-effects. However,
it is important to emphasize that in order to do this directly, the Subsecretariat would need to grow to



- 70 -
Annex 1.6
page 3 of 6
undesirable pr,portions, resulting in bureaucratization and inflexibility. Other procedures need to be
devised, which definitely means coming up with a different concept of the operation of the Subsecretariat
of Ecology.
In order to make the system of 'command and control" more effective, we would need
increasingly larger budgets, and these are not covered under the policy that we are going to have to
adhere to in the future. However, in the face of such handicaps, it is essential to emphasize that
environmental management in Mexico has gained highly valuable experience through covering the first
phase of enforcement of the General Law for Ecological Balance and Environmental Protection. Dozens
of technicians have been trained, and have maintained a significant response capacity which has prevented
pollution from creating even more problems than the ones we are faced with today. We are ready to
enter a new age in which responsibility for preserving the quality of the environment will be shared on
a more equal basis by different strata of Mexican society.
PRINCIPAL OBJECTIVES OF THE INSTITUTIONAL TRANSFORMATION
In essence, it is indispensable to expand the array of processes used to measure pollutant
emissions or to gauge the damage to natural resources. In order to meet the challenges arising from the
changes in strategy that are envisaged, the following measures are being planned:
1.    In accordance with the Law, bringing deconcentration to the Secretariat's delegations and
decentralization to the state and municipal governments.
2.    Transferring a significant portion of the functions of direct environmental auditing to private
laboratories. In principle, this illustrates the goal of ensuring that productive activities undergo
continuous environmental review, but in a process that is largely carried out by private
institutions. Clearly this idea will need to be promoted among private institutions, both national
as well as foreign.
3.    Giving the Subsecretariat the capacity to certify environmental auditing laboratoties. At present,
no such work is being carried out. More complex laboratory facilities as well as more highly
trained staff will need to be available.
4.    Giving tie Subsecretariat the responsibility of supervising, on a contingency basis, businesses and
private environmental auditing laboratories. Only part of this work is being done, but a different
stnrcture would be required.
5.    Considerably expanding the regulatory capacity that exists today. For this purpose, the data
banks on the pollution sources present in the national territory would be expanded, illustrating
a strategy that is both technical and political, so that the corresponding standards could be
established.
6.    Installing the internal capacity needed to keep tabs on scientific and technological advances that
could have an impact on the sector. Tnis means haviig a permanent capacity f 'r continual
updating of the environmental auditing laboratories.



- 71 -
Annex 1.6
page 4 of 6
7.    Reorganizing the structure of the SubsecretariLt of Ecology so that it is reconciled to the needs
implied in the earlier points. This restructuring would be carried out with the collaboration of
the interested personnel, under the direction of a recognized consulting firm hired for this
purpose.
8.    Without causing it to lose its responsive capacity, modernizing the ecology sector in order to
generate an entire system of economic incentives that promote participation by the social and the
private sectors in the fight against pollution, as well as in the surveillance of rational use of
natural resources.
The process involved in implementing the eight measures proposed above will bring new agents
into environmental management in the country. These will include state and municipal governmental
structures, thus releasing SEDUE from various of the responsibilities it holds in those entities today, as
well as the state universities, which will take the initial steps to provide academie support for
environmental management in their home regions. The result will be improved coverage and, as a result,
improved environmental capacity in the country.
The early results have been very encouraging. The governments of Jalisco and Tamaulipas, in
accordance with their respective state laws, are preparing to inaugurate environmental management
structures with their corresponding budgets. For its part, the academic community has initiated important
activities for the training programs, and has expressed its willingness to direct some of its research
structures toward working with difficult local problems.
In summary, in order to deal with environmental problems that cover practically the entire
national territory, the Subsecretariat will evolve a structure to progressively reduce its role in directly
handling these matters.
SOME IMPLICATIONS
In taking the political decision to become a second-tier service institution, the Subsecretariat of
Ecology is entering a two-phase transformation process: the first phase, lasting six months, will consist
of a detailed analysis of how the move will be made from its current structure to one that reflects the
stated objectives; the second phase will be the actual installation of the new structure.
The Subsecretariat of Ecology will not give up its present capacity for rapid response, to ensure
that the change does not undermine its essential ability to react quickly if needed. However, there are
even greater possibilities. The private sector could participate, by working within the framework of
economic incentives created by environmental enterprises on the one hand, and by sharing the work of
keeping records on pollutants on the other. The plan Is to license private laboratories and consulting
firms to perform a number of the tasks which SEDUE carries out on a limited scale today. The cost of
these services will be borne by the businesses that are inspected. The governmental authority will ask
each business to contract with a private concern to provide it with environmental auditing services which
record the pollutants it generates, as well as the volume of the emissions. Tle environmental authority
will receive the correspondinig reports so that it can take the measures that are called for.



- 72 -
Annex 1.6
page 5 of 6
In this way, we have conceived of a system in which private environmental auditing firms provide
businesses with a service that keeps them informed about the pollutants they are generating. The
respective cost will be -rne by the industry or service establishment, which will need to keep records
on its pollutants. The government, at its three levels, will be responsible for monitoring the reliability
of this process. With regard to the flow of resources, the government will collect the corresponding taxes
and funds for restoring the quality of the air, the soil, or the water.
The participation of private initiative will bring in another agent that can provide the coverage
we need. However, both SEDUE as well as the new environmental management structures of the state
governments will have to establish a well-thought-out strategy for certifying the capacity of the next
private environmental auditing firms. At present this function is only being carried out on a rudimentary
basis, but the necessary mechanisms are in the process of being designed.
We are looking for a new concept of environmental management that is more flexible and
efficient, which should simplify the processes involved in regulating this area. The essential feature of
the system we have decided to create is the involvement of various sectors in taking responsibility for
preserving the quality of the environment. The operational resources needed will not create an increased
fiscal burden because the responsibility for bearing the cost of any damage that is produced will fall on
the shoulders of those who are generating the pollution and degrading natural resources in an irrational
manner.
The reorganization of the Subsecretariat will be oriented around the goal of installing an
administrative system which is structured according to the concepts of total quality control. As a result,
an information system will be used for decision-making, as an essential element of this type of public
management, and this will in addition provide a tool for control as well as staff motivation. For this
purpose, a computer network will be installed which will connect up with all of the important control
points.
The information system will be paired with the appropriate devices for handling various types of
images. Using such mechanisms in company with geographical information techniques will make it
possible to display maps, satellite pictures, monitoring networks, etc. The aim here is to provide the
authorities with timely, sufficient, and simplified information management. Nonetheless, the main
purpose is to give the public access, at special unrestricted locations, to updated information on our most
pressing pollution problems, as well as on the progress being made in dealing with a variety of Issues.
The idea behind these measures is to change SEDUE's image to that of an institution that is largely
characterized by its openness and the objectivity of its information.
In addition, the information system will be vital to controlling the operation of the environmental
auditing laboratories. In fact, we are looking for instruments that will ensure that we can quickly resolve
any of the various flaws that mAy crop up in the laboratories, or in the governmental agencies at the other
two governmental levels. This represents a rapid response structure, similar to the one that the
Subsecretariat has in place today for handling other concems.
This entire transformation process will need to be carried out with special care. We will not
move forward until we are convinced that no potentially useful element is going to be destroyed. We can
expect some resistance, given the widespread perception that the authorities should be absolutely and
personAly committed to protecting the environment. This has led to direct control by SEDUE becoming



- 73 -
Annex 1.6
page 6 of 6
an absolute dogma in the minds of the public. A special promotion effort will be ind.spensable to gaining
citizen support for these new ideas on institutional strengthening.
In accordance witd the spirit of the Law, the "polluter pays principle" emphasizes prevention
based on payment for any damage that is caused. However, at the same time, this calls for increased
capacity to measure a wide range of parameters. Monitoring, which would always provide SEDUE with
basic information on a mandatory basis, requires the use of instruments that are based on the latest
technological advances. It is possible that the prices of certain products would reflect the impact of the
costs associated with environmental auditing. However, the increase could be borne by the actual
consumer, under the same principle.
Undeniably, many questions about this subject will need to be answered in the days to come. For
this reason, a special group of financial authorities in the country has been set up to analyze these
concerns. The institutional transformation will gradually move toward an environmental policy, which
will look for the necessary resources. These will not be meant to increase the budget of the new
Subsecretariat; when it comes to fiscal allocation, there may be a direct increase. The main investment
will be used to decrease pollution levels, and to provide an incentive to environmental firms which will
have the opportunity to pursue an attractive business.
It is also important to highlight the fact that a transformation of the kind that is envisaged will
instill ecological policy with a sense of equality, since those who are responsible for ecological damage
will have a clear responsibility in the eyes of the law and the citizenry. It is to be expected that the latter
group will collaborate more in programs which the Subsecretariat should be able to promote before the
deconcentrated and decentralizei organizations.  For their part, the authorities will create an
administrative approach that is more efficient and less discretionary.
The Subsecretariat of Ecology will have new duties which it does not perform today. It will
install the necessary structure to certify the capacity of the new agents: laboratories, study offices,
consulting firms, etc. This task will require more highly trained personnel and more advanced
laboratories. New inspection capabilities will need to be developed in order to check on the reliability
of the enviromnental auditing carried out by private concerns, as well as to compare the business's reports
on the measures taken to control environmental degradation. This work will need to be done on a
contingency basis, with an implicit policy of confidentiality which is accompanied by severe penalties for
any irregularities or cases of corruption. We will 'so need to work, with broad industrial participation,
to improvs tha design of standards that will not only put our productive system in a better position to
protect the environment, but also allow it to compete internationally, using cleaner technologies that
produce better quality products. Finally, our new duties will include being on the lookout for scientific
and technological advances in the ecology sector. This aspect is vital if we want our country to be a
strong competitor in the global market, where clean technologies will give an added boost to the volume
of sales.

MEXICO
Environmental Project
Coordination Agreement Between SEDUE and SARI-I
The coordination agreement between the Secretariat of Agriculture and Water
Resources and the Secretariat of Urban Development and Ecology, represented respectively
by the incumbents, Professor Carlos Hank Gonzalez and Mr. Patricio Chirinos Calero,
whose purpose is to operate the national water quality monitoring network on a coordinated
basis, the parties to be referred to respectively as "SARH" and "SEDUE" for the purposes of
this document, pursuant to the following declarations,
STATES THAT
In accordance with the Organic Law of the Federal Public Administration, the
Secretariat of Agriculture and Water Resources is an agency of the Federal Executive
Branch in charge of: "administering, controlling, and regulating the exploitation of
national waters, basins and water sources, as well as the corresponding federal zones,
issuing permits, authorizations, allocations or concessions for the exploitation and use
of national waters; participating in the programming, projections, construction,
adminiistration, operation, and maintenance of projects undertaken to supply water to
population centers; and, among others, carrying out and keeping up-to-date the
inventory of national water resources."
On January 16, 1989, a Decree was published in the Official Gazette by which the
National Water Commission is created as an administrative entity, independent from
the Secretariat of Agriculture and Water Resources. The National Water Commission
is in charge of: "proposing the country's water policy, formulating and updating the
Nationial Water Program, and implementing the Water Programming System; setting
up the criteria and guidelines to provide congruence to the Federal Government's
water-related programs and actions; establishing and, if necessary, proposing the
coordination agreement for actions to be taken by the administrative units and public
institutions which deal with water issues; administering and regulating, according to
the law, the national waters, water infrastructure and the resources devoted to the
latter; programming, evaluating, constructing, operating and maintaining water works
and carrying out actions which require complete water exploitation; ensuring and
monitoring the congruence between water-related programs and the allocation of
public resources for their implementation; and, among others, evaluating, proposing
and implementing, as necessary, the financial measures conducive to the development
of the country's water infrastructure and services.
II.   In accordance with the Organic Law of the Federal Public Administration, the
Secretariat of Urban Development and Ecology is an agency of the Federal Executive
Branch in charge of: "formulating and directing the general policies for human



Annex 1.7
75                    Page 2 of 3
settlements, urbanism, housing and ecology; establishing the conditions, ecological
criteria, and standards that must be met before wastewaters are discharged into the
collection networks, watersheds, channels, basins, and other water deposits and flows,
as well as the soil and subsoil, in order to prevent pollution that poses a threat to
public health or degrades the ecological systems; and in addition setting the technical
ecological criteria and standards that govern the use and allocation of natural
resources to preserve the quality of the environment and to monitor compliance and
evaluate the environmental impact of water works."
Under the terms of the General Law for Ecological Balance and Environmental
Protection, in coordination with the Secretariat of Agriculture and Water Resources
and with the participation, as necessary, of the Secretariat of Health, in accordance
with other laws, they shall carry out systematic and continuous monitoring of water
quality to detect the presence of pollutants or excess organic waste and implement the
appropriate measures or, as necessary, promote their execution.
Based on the above statements and on articles 35 and 37 of the Organic Law of the Federal
Public Administration, articles 1, 2, 8, 17, 30, 31, and 157-Bis of the Federal Waters Law;
and articles 90, 91, and 93 of the General Law for Ecological Balance and Environmental
Protection, the parties establish the following:
CLAUSES
FIRST.- The purpose of this document is to establish the coordination agreement whereby
"SARH," via the National Water Commission, and SEDUE, according to their respective
spheres of competence and working in coordination with each other, operate the national
water quality monitoring network and optimize its operation.
SECOND.- In 1992 "SEDUE" will complete a study directed toward redesigning the
network which will include the relocation of sampling sites, frequency of sampling,
parameters to be analyzed, field and laboratory procedures, and techniques for quality control
and interpretation of results.
The terms of reference and the evaluation of the preliminary and final reports on the study
will be prepared on a coordinated basis by "SARH," via the National Water Commission,
and "SEDUE." The final report should satisfy both.
THMIIRD.- Based on the study referred to in the preceding clause, in 1992, "SARH," via the
Nlational Water Commissioni, and SEDUE will identify the sampling sites which they should
operate according to their respective areas of compeiknce.
FOURTH.- "SARH," via the National Water Commission, and SEDUE will make the
necessary provisions to obtain the human resources and materials they need in order to fulfill
their respective monitoring programs.



A-nnex_1..7
- 76 -                   Page 3 of 3
FIF'FH- The sampling sites operated by each agency will be complementary, and together
they will constitute the National Monitoring Network.
SIXTH.- Each agency will establish an internal quality control program for the taking,
handling, and transport of samples and laboratory analyses. The two control programs will
be similar, and will be agreed upon on art ainual basis by the parties involved.
The methods used to analyze water quality will be standardized so that laboratory results can
be compared. "SARH," via the National Water Commission, will furnish a study prep,ared
for this purpose.
SEVENTH.- Both agencies wiil standardize their computer equipment and procedures in
order to create one single water quality information system. "SARH," via the National
Water Commission, through the Office oRn Quality, Water Recycling, and Environmental
Impact, will make the information system on water quality available to the General
Directorate for the Prevention and Control of Environmental Pollution of "SEDUE"; the
system will be used to store, process, and analyze the information that both agencies obtain
from their monitoring activities.
Only these two agencies will have free access to the system, and they will be committed to
storing the information withini a period not to exceed three months and transmitting it to each
other via MODEM.
EIGHTH.- During 1992 each agency will analyze the location and capacity of its regional
laboratories so that these laboratories do not overlap unless the capacity envisaged for one or
more of them ii not sufficient to provide services to the other agency. In this case, the
institution concemed will establish its own complemnentary installations.
NINTH.- Through a timely exchange of their work programs, the two agencies will ensure
that their water quality studies are complementary.
Read and ratified by the parties involved, the present coordination agreement is signed in
Mexico City, D.F., on this the 23rd day of the month of March 1992,

troduction
As the result of a policy aimed at protecting the environment and at the
same time promoting the country'o economic development, a series of laws,
regulationa, and standards has been issued which make it possible to
rationally administer the country's natural reoources. Therefore, in order to
obtain authorization for the execution of any type of work which could cause
ecological imbalance, an environmental impact assessment must be presented
which identifiet and assesses the possible harm to the ecosystem, as
stipulated under the General Law on Ecological Balance and Environmental
Protection. Thus, it has been recorded in the seven subsections of Article 29
that the federal government, through SEDUE, shall be responsible for
evaluating environmental impact in the following areas:
I.    Federal public works;
11.   Hydraulic works, general communication routes, oil pipelineb, gas
pipelines, and coal pipelines;
III. The chemical, petrochemical, iron and steel, paper, sugar, beverage,
cement, automotive, and electrical power generation and transmission
industries;
IV.   The exploration, extraction, treatment, and refining of mineral
substances that are the property of the federation;
V.    Federal tourist developments;
VI.   Installations for the treatment, storage or elimination of radioactive
waste; and
VII. Forest utilization of tropical forests and jungles and of hard-to-
regenerate species, in those cases set forth in the recond paragraph of
Article 56 of the Forest Law.
In turn, the Internal Regulations of the Secretariat for Urban
Development and Ecology, in Article 23, Subsection II, give sole authority to
the General Directorate of Ecological Standards and Regulation for
determining, in the area of environmental impact, the authorization of
proposed public or private works or activities, as stipulated in Article 29 of
the Law and Article 5 of its Regulations on Environmental Impact, while SEDUE
empowers the state delegations to evaluate environmental impact as stipulated
in Article 37, Subsection XXIX of the aforementioned Internal Regulations.
Frequently the petitioners present their environmental impact assessment
to the SEDUE delegation within the state where they plan to carry out their
project and meek authorization there because they are unaware that the
delegation, under the Internal Regulations of SEDUE, has no power to authorize
or reject the project. Subsequently it is sent on to Mexico City, to the
SEDUE central offices, so that the General Directorate of Ecological Standards
and Regulation can evaluate It and reach the appropriate decision according to
the law.



- 78 -
Annex 1.8
page 2 of 5
It in also trje that eevere limitations on human and techvical reoources
are apparent in the SEDUE delegations. In the specific case of environmental
lmpact assessment, thia shortage is critical due to high degree of technical
specialization and interdisciplinary work that this subject requires.
There are thus two constraints which have an impact both on the
petitioner for authorization as well as on SEDUE:
1)    The time period for responding to the petitioner which io stipulated in
the Regulations on Environmental Impact of the General Law on Ecological
Balance and Environmental Protection is often exceeded.
2)    The lack of decision-making power, although not the sole factor, has
been behind the scant or nonexistent technical and administrative
structure of the SEDUE delegations when it comes to dealing with matters
of environmental impact.
There is therefore a clear need for a scheme of structural strengthening
of, and subsequently, delegation to, SEDUE's state delegations, with the goal
of meeting the demand for environmental impact authorizations, as well as
fully complying with the provisions and terms of the legislation that
regulates this area.
Objectives:
1)    To give the SEDUE's state delegations the power to appropriately issue
authorizations or rejections with regard to the environmental impact of
specific projects.
2)    To strengthen the technical and adminiatrative structure of SEDUE's
state delegations so that they can deal with matters of environmental
impact.
Strategy:
l)    Formulation of a list of projects whose environmental impact can be
analyzed and resolved by SEDUE's state delegations; in other words,
delegation from the central offices to the delegations.
2)    Establishment and prov±sion of training programs and courses for the
technical staff of SEDUE's state delegations.
3)    Strengthening of the response capacity of SEDUE's state delegations via
new hiring of specialized personnel.
4)    Preparation of a proposed modification of the SEDUE Internal Regulations
in which the SEDUE's state delegations are given the power to grant
authorizations in questions of environmental impact for certain
projects.
5)    Promotion of this modification of the SEDUE Internal Regulations before
the corresponding legal units.
6)    Definition of the program of decentralization activities.
Works or activities provided for under the legislation which must be
submitted for prior authorization by the federal government, acting through
SEDUE, in questions of environmental impact, but which could, subject to
analysis* be delegated from the central offices to SEDUE'. state delegatLons.

INTRODU ZTION
In recent decades Mexico has been experiencing accelerated processes of economic
development and settlement of its territory which have been accompanied by significant disruption of
its ecosystems. Soil erosion, deforestation, and similar processes of ecological degradation, as well as
the threatened or actual disappearance of wild species of flora and fauna, have created growing
concern at all national levels.
In order to confront these environmental problems, the government of the Republic has
directed its activities-through the National Development Plan, the National Environmental Protection
Program, and the General Law for Ecological Balance and Environmental Protection-toward dealing
with the country's most serious ecological imbalances.
Given the size and complexity of these ecological problems, solving them will call for well-
ordered and organized participation by the three governmental levels as well as the private and social
sector, supported by the institutional foundation of ecological policy.



- 86 -
Annex 1.9
page 5 of 20
I.    MEXICAN POLICY ON PROTECTED NATURAL AREAS
One of the principal challenges of the conservation effort has been the need to integrate the
aspects of protection and sustainable development of natural resources with the economic and
modernization processes necessary to the country's development.
In this sense, one of the primary strategies of Mexican environmental policy is the
consolidation of the National System of Protected Natural Areas (SINAP), which seeks to make
conservation of the country's representative ecosystems compatible with the need for rational use of
the country's natural resources, as a basis for sustained development of its communities. At the samne
tine, each of the protected natural areas incorporated (into the national system) itself becomes a
biological unit for the conservation of biodiversity, as well as an in situ bank of germ plasm.
The National Development Plan establishes a strategy for conservation of the country's
representative ecosystems and their natural resources in the particular case of protected natural areas.
This strategy calls for consolidation of the national system of protected natural areas through the
expansion of programs for the conservation, development, and administration of these areas;
diversification of their resource utilization, as well as rational and sustainable use of these resources
through appropriate techniques and methods of reproduction, propagation, and management; and, at
the same time, the creation of a system for ecological inspection and surveillance.
At the same time, the National Environmental Protection Program calls for the country's
economic evolution to encompass respect for nature, conservation of resources, and preservation of
the environment. One of its principal stated objectives is to ensure the recovery, protection, and
conservation of natural resources and the equilibrium of ecosystems; and the principal strategy it
proposes is to increase the area covered by the National System of Protected Natural Areas, and to
strengthen the capacity for protection and surveillance of the System's component areas.
In March 1988 the General Law for Ecological Balance and Environmental Protection went
into effect. Title 2 of this law has the purpose of legally establishing the categories, declarations, and
organization of protected natural areas. This article of the Law also includes a section on the
National System of Protected Natural Areas, and another on wild flora and fauna.
The important features of the above-cited Law are listed below:
The Law stipulates that the Federation, the states, and the municipalities shall establish
measures to protect natural areas, in such a way as to ensure the preservation and restoration of their
ecosystems, especially those that are most representative (Article 38); and it indicates that the natural
areas in the national territory can come under protection as ecological reserves and be govemed by
restrictions imposed by the competent authorities making these areas subject to only such utilization
and exploitation as are determined to be socially and nationally necessary (Article 44).
The Law considers these areas to be protected natural areas; their establishment is in the
public interest, and they have the following goals (A; uele 4;):



- 87 -
Annex 1.9
page 6 of 20
1.     To preserve natural settings that are representative of the different biogeographic and
ecological regions, and the most fragile ecosystems, in order to protect the equilibrium and
continuity of evolutionary and ecological processes.
H.    To safeguard the genetic diversity of wild species on which evolutionary continuity depends,
particularly those that are endemic, endangered, or close to extinction.
III.   To ensure rational utilization of ecosystems and their components.
IV.   To provide an area conducive to scientific research and the study of ecosystems and their
equilibrium.
V.    To generate information and technologies that make possible rational and sustained utilization
as well as preservation of the country's resources.
VI.   To protect settlements, communication routes, industrial installations, and areas of agricultural
development by establishing forest zones in mountains that are flood sources or working with
the hydrological cycle in basins, or by using any other approaches that help to protect the
surrounding elements which an area is ecologically linked with.
VII.  To protect the natural surroundings of areas that contain archaeological, historical, and artistic
monuments and remains of importance to the national culture and identity.
In order to encompass the full range of conditions, environments, and situations, SINAP
includes nine categories of protected natural areas:
1. Biosphere reserves;
2. Special biosphere reserves;
3. National parks;
4. Natural monuments;
5. National marine parks;
6. Areas for the protection of natural resources;
7. Areas for the protection of flora and fauna;
8. Urban parks;
9. Zones subject to ecological conservation.
Ihe areas included under sections 1-7 are considered to fall within the domain of the
Federation; in other words, they are to be established and administered by the federal government.
Those included under items 8 and 9 are to be local in nature, as are any that are so designated
according the corresponding state or municipal provisions (Article 46).
In addition, when it comes to establishing, administering, and developing protected natural
areas, local communities are considered to play a fundamental role in measures to protect the
ecosystems, through their participation in various programs for the protection, caretaking, and
recovery of resources.



- 88 -
Annex 1.9
page 7 of 20
The protected natural areas that are considered to fall within federal domain as a whole make
up the National System of Protected Natural Areas (SINAP), as stipulated in Article 76.
At presen., the system Is composed of 44 national parks, 8 biosphere reserves, 13 special
biosphere reserves, 1 area for the protection of wild flora and fauna, and I Natural Monument.
These areas are listed betow:

In addition, among its other objectives SINAP aims to enforce the provisions of the General
Law for Ecological Balance and Environmental Protection with reference to protected natural areas in
order to preserve the natural heritage of the nation, coordinate with the appropriate provisions of the
federal public administration in the areas under its jurisdiction, and coordinate with social and private
groups regarding specialized measures oriented around the goals which the natural areas in the
national system have been established to achieve.
Articles 48, 49, 50, 51, 52, 53, 54, 55, and 56 of this same Law define and categorize
protected natural areas, emphasizing the procedures and requirements they should all have with regard'
to biological importance, surface area, and the use and a'location of natural resources, among others.
Finally, in order to comply with the provisions and policies of the legislation in force, the
management of protected areas is planned through the preparation of management plans. Such plans
are categorized in the above-cited Law and serve as planning instruments that give the basic outlines
of what needs to be done in order to achieve the implicit objectives behind the establishment of a
natural protected area: preserving biodiversity and banks of germ plasm, and at the same time
promoting comprehensive community development.
II.    MANAGEMENT PLANS AS AN INSTRUMENT OF MEXICAN POLICY ON
PROTECTED .NATURAL AREAS
In line with the policies of the National Development Plan, the National Environmental
Protection Program, and the General Law for Ecological Balance and Environmental Protection, the
preparation of management plans is a fundamental stage of the planning process for protected natural
areas that are pa-rt of SINAP. These plans incorporate the programming of activities directed toward
the protection, conservation, and promotion of biodiversity and in situ banks of germ plasm; intra-
and inter-institutional coordination; and integration of the public, social, and private sectors into the
processes of conservation and rational utilization of the natural resources in these areas.
In this sense, tnanagement plans serve as a planning tool which uses knowledge about the
problems of a protectedi nattral area, as well as information about its natural resources and how they
are used, as a basis for proposals on the organization, classification, and coordination of measures
that will make it possible to achieve the objectives and targets established for that area. A
management plan is thus seen as a dynamic and flexible tool which is capable of gathering feedback
and adapting in accordance with the management policies and standards issued for that purpose, and



- 91 -
Annex 1.9
page 10 of 20
which always acknowledges the importance of fulfilling the objectives that the protected natural area
was established to achieve.
In this context, Article 65 of the General Law for Ecological Balance and Environmental
Protection stipulates that the agency or agencies of the federal executive branch who propose that a
protected natural area be established under federal jurisdiction (in this case, SEDUE) shall prepare the
management plan for that area, with the participation, as applicable, of any other competent agencies
and local authorities, within the stated time period in the corresponding declaration.
In Article 68, the above-cited Law indicates that the management plan for a reserve must
contain at least the following elements:
1.    A description of the area's physical, biological, social, and cultural features, in the
national, regional, and local context;
2.    The short-, medium-, and long-term actions *,r be taken, and how they are linked to
the National System for Democratic Planning. These actions will encompass
research, resource utilization, expansion, distribution, operation, coordination, follow-
up, and control;
3.    The specific objectives of the reserve; and
4.    The technical standards that apply, where appropriate, to the exploitation of flora and
fauna, as well as to sanitary, crop-related, and domestic cuttings, or any whose aim is
to prevent contamination of the soil and water,
HI.   THE CONTENTS OF A MANAGEMENT PLAN
The following is a description of the principal components of a management plan. It should
be noted that, since national parks and reserves have distinct management objectives, the management
plans for these areas will contain some differences as well. It is therefore noted in the text when
differences between these two types of areas occur.
1.    INTRODUCTION
1.1   Preliminary Information
The geographical location of the area is discussed in both physical and geopolitical terms,
including access routes and the area it occupies. There is also a brief description of the
general characteristics of the locale, which provides the rationale behind its conservation,
management, and sustainable development, along with an explanation of its importance in the
international, national, and regional context.



- 92 -
Annex 1.9
page 11 of 20
1.2    Background
This is a brief description of the area which reviews the information available, the legal
aspects, previous conservation efforts, the current situation, and any general issues.
2.     OBJECTIVES
The general and specific objectives of the management plan are listed.
3.     ASSESSMENT
This section describes the area's physical, biotic, socioeconomic, and cultural features, as well
as its problems.
3.1    Physical Characteristics
a) Physiography and topography
The area is put into context according to its physiographical region and province,
based on the classification system developed by J. Rzeclowski (1983). There is also a
list of such topographical features as ravines, rises, valleys, gullies, etc., in addition
to the names of its main formations, with maps and charts to back up all this
information.
b) Geology
The area is classified in terms of its geological roots and evolution, with emphasis on
its most important characteristics (volcanic activity, faults, types of rock, etc.)
c) Edaphology
Based on the FAO soil classification system, the specific features of each area are
listed, providing a profile of soil types.
d) Hydrology
The hydrological basins found in each protected area are looked at from a
hydrological viewpoint, including the principal streams, rivers, bodies of water, etc.
e) Climatology
The area is classified from a climatological perspective based on Kappen's
classification system, as modified by Garcfa E. (1981). Information is also included
on the closest meteorological season(s), if any.



- 93 -
Annex l.9
page 12 of 20
The climate data that can be classifled includes average monthly temperatures,
monthly rainfall, relatiNe humidity, and the hottest and wettest months during the
year.
3.2    Biotic Characteristics
a) Flora
The area is classified in terms of its prevailing types of vegetation and their most
important associations, with a description of the representative species in each one, as
well as any species that are endemic, close to extinction, rare, or endangered.
b) Fauna
In this section, the area is classified in terms of its fauna, with a description of the
most important vertebrate or invertebrate species, how they are associated with
ecological units and habitats, and which species are endemic, rare, endangered, or
close to extinction.
3.3    Socioeconomic Characteristics
'This aspect involves an examination of the socioeconomic conditions of the
communities present in the area or its zone of influence, and of how these
communities use its natural resources. Particular attention is paid to the following:
Property rights
Current soil use
Here, using supplementary information from the draft Land Tenancy Law under
preparation by the Secretariat, current land uses are indicated (for agriculture,
livestock raising, hunting, mining, tourism, and other purposes), along with
information on land regulation.
3.4    Cultural Characteristics
The protected natural area is classified based on the presence of any archaeological
remains of historic or cultural importance. This includes their state of preservation,
importance, etc., as well as the historical or cultural significance of the area where
they are found.
3.5    Rationale
Here the value and potential uses of both national parks and reserves are specified
according to the criteria in the following paragraphs:



- 94 -
A_nn_U
page 13 of 20
a) ecological Importance
The biological importance of the area is described in terms of the diversity of its
biological components, ecosystems, and genetic material or its genetic diversity; the
representativity of its existing ecosystems; the degree of preservation of those
ecosystems; the preservation of germ plasm because of the presence of species that are
endemic, rare, endangered, close to extinction, or of particular interest; and the
features and break-down of the area's plant substratum.
b) historical and cultural importance (if any)
c) scientific, educationai, and recreational importance
In this category, the area is looked at in terms of its value as an area for research on
the ecosystems and their components; for recreational purposes; and for the
development of environmental education programs. At the same time, the area's
social impact is pointed out with regard to the potential number of visitors, the
creation of jobs, etc.
3.6   issues
Based on the rationale and assessment, in this category the problems, limitations, and
restrictions that hinder conservation in the area are described, along with any
obstacles to the utilization of area resources.
4.    INVESTMENT PROGhAM OR ANNUAL OPERATING PROGRAM
This category details the short-, medium-, and long-term (six-year) investment program
needed in order to carry out the measures stipulated in the management plan for the protected
natural area, in complete correspondence with its horizontal and vertical breakdown and
subcomponents.
5.    SUBCOMPONENTS OF THE MANAGEMENT PLAN FOR A PROTECTED NATURAL
AREA
The following is a list of the principal subcomponents that must be included in any
management plan or investment program for a protected natural area:
5.1    Conservation Component
5.1.1    Subcomponent on the prevention, control, and fighting of forest fires
Contains measures and strategies for controlling forest fires which are aimed at
mitigating their negative impact on stands of trees and keeping fires from spreading,
as well as taking the steps necessary to prevent outbreaks.



- 95 -
AnneL1.2
page 14 of 20
5.1.2    Subcomponent on the prevention, control, and flghting of forest plagues
Includes the rapid detection of forest plagues, based on the use of existing measures
and strategies to prevent the loss of large tree populations and prevent the spread or
development of plagues by strengthening forest stands.
5.1.3    Subcomponent on reforestation
Encompasses measures and strategies that favor the recovery of plant cover and
protection of the soil in areas of erosion, and among tree populations that cannot
regenerate themselves by natural means.
5.1.4    Subcomponent on soil protection and recovery
Includes the detection of high-risk areas for erosion, as well as areas that are already
affected, in order to determine the techniques that need to be employed in order to
recover these areas within a given time period.
5.1.5    Subcomponent on protection of cultural resources
Includes measures and strategies for preserving the cultural heritage in protected
natural areas.
5.1.6    Subcomponent on management and protection of wild fauna
Involves preserving diversity in the natural area and protecting the eco!ogically
representative wild fauna that live there, in order to niaintain equilibrium in the
ecosystems present in the area.
5.1.7    Subcomponent on seedbeds
Includes measures and strategies for preserving the ecological diversity of the natural
area by protecting and propagating the germ plasm of the dominant and ecologically
representative plant species which are adapted to the environmental conditions found
there, and for maintaining a supply of the seedlings necessary for reforestation
activities.
5.1.8    Subcomponent on breeding sites
Includes measures and strategies for preserving the ecological diversity of the area by
improving the numbers and distribution of the ecologically representative wild fauna
that live there.



- 96 -
page 15 of 20
5.2    Public Use and Recreation Component
5.2.1     Subcomponent on tourism
Includes strategies and measures for providing the visiting public with recreational and
leisure facilities in contact with nature without affecting the different ecological
components of the area.
5.2.2     Subcomponent on sign posting
Sets forth strategies and measures for providing visitors with orientation on the
various services that are available, as well as any restrictions that may apply during
their stay in the area.
5.2.3     Subcomponent on environmental education
Includes guidelines for providing the elements necessary to sensitize visitors to the
Importance of conserving the area's natural resources.
5.2.4     Subcomponent on environmeiital interpretation
Includes strategies and measures that make it possible to maintain installations,
mechanisms, and activities that involve visitors in the conservation effort for the
natural area.
5.2.5     Subcomponent on research
Sets forth strategies and measures for making available technical and scientific
elements that permit rational and sustainable management of the area's natural
resources.
5.2.6     Subcomponent on infrastructure
Sets forth strategies and measures for maintaining the installations necessary to
adequately fulfill the objectives of conservation, protection, and development of the
area's natural resources, in addition to providing recreation and attending to the needs
of the visiting public.
5.3    Administrative Component
5.3.1     Subcomponent on operation
Sets forth strategies and measures for coordinating and implementing the activities,
measures, programs, and projects of the natural area and the personnel who work
there.



-    - 7L
\                                                           bml~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~nex, 19
page 16 of 20
i;            5.3.2     Subcomponent on inspection and surveillance
Sets forth strategies and measures for strengthening the protection of natural resources
through activities aimed at reducing the incidence of unlawful and careless use of
those resources.
5.3.3    Subcomponent on zoning
Establishes guidelines for delimiting and setting boundaries to divide the area's surface
into specific zones, based on soil use and capability, where any activities carried out
are subject to regulation.
5.4    Coordination Component
5.4.1    Subcomponent on inter-institutional coordination
Includes measures and strategies for making arrangements and agreements with the
three governmental levels to join efforts and resources in the implementation of the
management plan for the area.
5.4.2    Subcomponent on community coordination
Includes strategies and measures for promoting the effort to sensitize the communities
that reside in the area and raise their level of ecological, cultural, and educational
awareness, so that agreements can be drawn up for community participation in the
recovery, conservation, and sustainable exploitation of the area's natural and cultural
resources.
5.4.3    Subcomponent on scientific coordination
Includes measures and strategies for fostering, organizing, and promoting participation
by the scientific community in the execution of research projects in the areas of
evaluation, recovery, and conservation of natural resources, as well as in the conduct
of socioeconomic research involving the common lands and agrarian communities
located within the protected area's zone of influence.
5.4.4    Subcomponent on coordination with non-governmental organizations
Includes measures and strategies for promoting and maintaining support from the
different social sectors.
'5.5    Legal Framework Component
5.5.1    Subcomponent or the internal regulation of the area
Encompasses preparation of tne set of measures and policies that must be complied
with when any activity is carried out within the protected area.



- 98 -
Ann-x I
page 17 of 20
5.5.2    Subcomponent on delimitation and marking of boundaries
Includes measures to physically define the borders of the protected area with boundary
stones and monuments In order to conserve its natural resources.
5.5.3    Subcomponent on land tenancy
Encompasses the determination of regulations governing land ownership in the
protected area, as well as the specification of compensation to be paid for any
subsequent damages.
5.5.4    Subcomponent on concessions
Encompasses measures and strategies for licensing installations and the provision of
services to the private and social sector in such a way as to ensure that these obtain
benefits from providing the licensed service and bring in financial resources that make
the operation of the protected area self-supporting.
6.    Annexes

6.2    List of Fauna

and longitude), access routes, and important nearby population centers.

vegetation.
6.4    Bibliography, together with research and support work on the area.

The World Bank
Washington, D.C.
I am sending you the following pages in order to clarify certain points with respect to the document
"Protected Natural Areas of Mexico, Legislation and Management Plans," which was sent to you on 2
December 1992.
this letter will help to give you a better understanding of that paper discussing Mexican policy on
protected natural areas.
A.    LOCAL COMMUNITIES
When the management plan for each area is being prepared, the local communities will
participate and be consulted during the analysis of socioeconomic and cultural concerns of importance
to the plan, and will also help to identify the specific coordination strategies and measures required in
order to implement the management plan for the area in question.
.At this stage, it is inadvisable to establish any partial commitments with the communities
residing in the area, beyond those that have already been mentioned. 7This is to ensure that the
coordination that goes on reflects the overall perspective of the actual management plan once it has
been defined, and that, at the same time, the national strategy of coordination brigades is used, as
described in the policy paper under Section 5.4.2.
Once the management plan has been set up, more formal coordination can be implemented,
along with any improvements that appear necessary in light of the experience gained during this
coordination process. This will also help to ensure that the management plan remains a dynamic one.
B.    CURRIRNT DELIMITATION OF PROTECTED NATURAL AREAS
Although the process of formulating management plans will yield useful information for
updating the current boundaries of natural protected areas and defining their surface area, any
prospective changes must result from the overall perspective of the National System of Protected
Natural Areas, and must at the same time take into account the possibility of integrating biological
corridors.



100-
Annex 1.9
page 19 of 20
As a result, this aspect needs to be appmoached in a systematic and integrated fashion, based
on nat}onai priorities and the findings of study projects incorporated into the Environmental Sectoral
Credit which deal with the restructuring of SINAP and the creation of biological corridors.
C.    ENVIRONMENTAL IMPACT STUDIES
Here I would like to discuss the procedure for evaluating the potential environmental impact
of any basic Infrastructure that migMt be established in Mexico's protected natural areas as part of the
GEF project In Mexico-such as surveillance booths, administrative buildings, seedbeds, breeding
sites, guided trails, etc.-and which is meant to be used in the management and administration of
protected natural areas and the biodiversity they contain.
On this subject, I would like to point out to you that a management plan is in and of itself a
national instrument for ecological organization and the evaluation of any potential environmental
impact that might result from works or activities for the management, conservation, and exploitation
of the natural resources that make up the biodiversity of a protected natural area.
At the sanme time, I would like to inform you that the General Directorate of Ecological
Conservation of Natural Resources--a division of the Subsecretariat which is under my charge--is the
unit responsible for evaluating management plans and the works, activities, and measures that are
planned as part of them, as well as for setting technical criteria and standards aimed at mitigating
their potential impact on the environment.
D.    TRAINING FOR PERSONNEL IN PROTECTED NATURAL AREAS
Personnel training is unquestionably an extremely important aspect of the management of
protected natural areas, and constitutes a comprehensive program for all the areas in the system.
However, we feel that it is inappropriate and economically wasteful to have various, unconnected
programs in the different areas. For this reason, the Subsecretariat has a general program to cover
the training needs of all the areas.
In fact, this program is based on the availability of highly trained personnel, some coming
from central offices and others from the delegations, who have broad experience, on the one hand,
and who have in addition attended courses for refresher training provided through international
programs. These include the course on reserves which has been organized each year in conjunction
with US-FWS, DUMAC, the Technological Institute of Monterrey, and specialized North American
organizations. There is a similar meeting on park management in which the US-NPS, WWF, and the
Antonio Narro Autonomous University talke part. The program has been repeated for five years.
The corresponding General Directorate has 18 persons who have participated in this event. Recently
specific training in fire management has been included. These trained personnel serve in turn as
training monitors for the remaining staff. We do not feel that this process should be broken down
area by area and incorporated into the management plans.



101 -
AnkI2l.
page 20 of 20
E.    INDICATORS FOR EVALUATION
The indicators for evaluation that appk,, - tl the areas have been reported to the Bank under
separate cover.
The, specific indicators for each area need to be determined in light of its definitive
management plan, and based on the opinion of the technical advisory council that is associated with
that specific area. They also need to be flexible so that they can be modified as part of the
Implementation process. The Barnk already has a foundation in this area.
In the hope that the Bank finds these details useful for its purposes, I remain,

MEXICO
Environmental Project
Terms of Reference for the Redesion of the
National Water Ouality Monitoring Network
to be O_erated by SEDUEZSE and CNA
This document definee the terms of reference to which the proposals for
carrying out the study known as "Redesign of the National Water Quality
Monitoring Network" must conform.
I.    General Backaround
Water is a liquid that is vital to a variety of socioeconomic activities
which are carried out in the country's human communities. It is also the
direct support of the aquatic life which ranges from the one-celled organisms
all the way up to the blue whale, the largest of the aquatic mammals.
Water has an important material as well as emotional value. It is a
resource whose presence can define success or failure for the peasant. In the
city it is used in the preparation of food and drink. Industrial plants mix
water with their products during their manufacturing processes, or use it as a
coolant.
Obviously ships need water in order to sail. Water is avidly sought for
recreation (swimming, rowing, and fishing), while land with a sea view is
appreciated for whatever social purpose.
Temporary considerations need to be added to the above description due
to the fact that water is changing in accordance with complicated patterns
which are influenced by both natural and artificial circumstances. An a
result, there is rarely enough water for all these uses in terms of quantity,
quality, and ideal locality.
Estimates from the National Hydraulic Plan make it clear that Mexico has
a sufficient volume of water to meet the supply needs of all sectors; however,
its geographical distribution is completely adverse for almost half the
national territory. In addition, as in other countries, Mexico is facing the
problem of water pollution generated by the production of wastes in urban,
industrial, and agricultural and livestock areas, which have a variety of
effects on our bodies of water, and in consequence affect aquatic ecosystems
and the various uses of water.
Thus in order to protect and, as appropriate, improve water quality so
that it is usable for all desired purposes, it is necessary to observe and
measure the changes and trends in water quality. For this reason, in 1975 the
federal government created the National Water Quality Monitoring Network,
which is made up of a series of locations along the principal rivers, lakes or
reservoirs, estuaries, beaches, ports, aquifers, and coastal areas of
interest, from which water samples are taken and conveyed to laboratories so
that they can be analyzed and their types of components identified.
In order to obtain information that would support the environmental
management activities which SEDUE is supposed to carry out in the country as a
result of its assigned functions under the current laws, in 1985 SEOUS was
incorporated into water quality monitoring, operating 108 stations, most of



- 110 -                          Annex 2.2
page 2 of 5
which coincided with those that had existed up until that time.
SEDUE's operation of the monitoring network has run up against a variety
of administrative ao well as technical problems which have led the General
Directorate for the Prevention and Control of Environmental Pollution to
propose a study called "Redesign of the National Water Quality Monitoring
Network," to be carried out on a contractual basis. The study's aim is to
improve the structure and operation of the network under the direction of the
state SEDUE delegations, so that the results of the measurements allow the
authorities in charge of water quality to make the appropriate decisions to
protect and/or improve it.
II.   Obiectives
The fundamental objective of the proposed study is to obtain maximum
efficiency in the operation of the current monitoring network run by the
Secretariat of Urban Development and Ecology and the National Water
Commission.
In addition it is proposed:
To define--in keeping with the specific functions of each unit--the
placement of stations, the frequency of sampling, the pararmieters to be
measured, the operative systems, and the approach to be used to coordinate the
handling of the information obtained.
III. General Inatructions
The proposals to carry out work under the present terms of reference
should be delivered in duplicate to the offices of the General Directorate for
the Prevention and Control of Environmental Pollution, which are located at
Rio Elba No. 20 - ler Piso, Col. Cuauht6moc, C.P. 06500, Mexico, D.P.
The proposals should contain the following: a detailed description of
the activities and tasks that will be carried out; a budget, broken down by
activities and costs; a timetable of execution; and a list of the technical
personnel involved, with their professional background and the tasks they will
perform during the study.
IV.   Technical Specifications
The execution of the different phases of the study must conform to the
following specificationsa:
1.    Collection and analysis of information
An analysis is to be made of all aspects of Mexican state and federal
legislation that touch on the question of environmental management, in
particular: the organic Law of Federal Public Administration, the General Law
on Ecological Balance and Environmental Protection, the Federal Water Law, the
Sanitary Code, and the Law on Sanitary Engineering, as well as the laws and
regulations on environmental questions which have been issued by the state
governments.
The above io directed towtrd identifying the specific water quality
monitoring duties of various fecleral units, such as SEDUE, SARH, and SEDEMAR.
Information should be collected on the structure and operation of the



page 3 of 5
Global Monitoring Network/Water of the World Health Organization; from other
countries, such as the United States and Canada in North America, and Brazil,
Chile, and Peru in South America, with the objective of making comparisons and
adopting, as appropriate, any useful aspects for the network in Mexico.
2.    Definition of Monitoring Objectiveo
Based on the information collected an in consultation with staff of
SEDUE and CNA, the consultanto should develop and define the specific
monitoring objectives for each institution. These monitoring objectives mum
respond to the legal responsibilities and operation requirements of each
institution, while at the same time avoiding duplication. The consultants mus
ensure that the recommended objectives have the agreement of both SEDUE and
CNA. The must be clearly stated, specify time limits and performance
indicators, and be given in a form that allows assessment of whether or not
they have been met.
3.    Parameters of quality
In accordance with the responsibilities of SEDUE and CNA identified
above, and based on the situation with regard to sources of pollution, types
of pollutants, potentially affected aquatic ecosystems, and risks to water
users, a number of basic parameters are to be determined which will always be
measured in the waters of the receiving bodies.
There also needs to be a list of special parameters which are defined
for each station, depending on its location with respect to pollution sources
and types of pollutants.
In selecting both the basic as well as the special parameters, thought
must be given to the analysis of samples of water, esediments, and biological
specimens. In addition, it is important to keep in mind the intrinsic
differences between continental and marine waters.
4.    Frequency
In accordance with the information analyzed, the necessary frequency of
measuremet for the parameters of water quality should be determined, thus
defining the sampling frequency.
The minimum frequency for measuring the basic parameters is to be
defined; in other words, the parameters that should always be measured in the
different types of water, in addition to the frequency of measurement for the
special parameters.
5.    Measurement, sampling, and analysis in the field
Techniques should be established for the staff of the field brigades to
use in collecting samples, carrying out field analyses, and, as applicable,
measuring the flow of currents or the volume of lakes or reservoirs.
Recommendations should be made for techniques that will keep a sample's
characteristics representative and reliable until they are analyzed in the
laboratory.
Due to the fact that there are well-known differences between the
characteristics of continental as opposed to marine water, it is necessary to
define and recommend specific parameters and analysis techniques for each type
of sample that has been identified for inclusion in the monitoring.
Recommendations are to made as to the appropriate equipment,



- 112 -
page 4 of 5
instruments, materials, and reagents fo.? the taking, packing, and transport of
samples to the laboratory, in addition to field record forms.
6.    Laboratory analysis
The consultants will compile a list of water quality variables that must
be analyzed in the laboratory, and recommend detection limits and analytical
methods that will meet the objectives of the program. The consultant will
also calculate, on a monthly basis, the type and number of teats that must be
done by basin.
7.    The Private Sector Option
Consistent with SEDUE's institutional strategy, its portion of the water
quality monitoring program (from sampling to laboratory analysis) may be
contracted to private sector firms. The consultants should therefore examine
this option, recommend how best to implement it, and calculate the likely
costs of this approach. The consultants should also, in close consultation
with staff of SEDUE and CNA, define the role that SEDUE and CNA laboratories
would play under this approach and the physical facilities, equipment,
reagents, and human and financial resources that their central and regional
laboratories would need in order to play this role.
S.    Data management
As water quality monitoring produces an enormous quantity of data,
recommendations need to be made as to the way in which this data is to be
condensed for inclusion in the data base to be operated by SEDUE and CNA.
The consultants should recommend the best alternative means of operating
a common SEDUE/CNA data base. This assessment should include the need for
personnel--including professional profiles--as well as materials and equipment
for computerized information management, with the goal of producing reports on
water quality that can placed at the disposal of users of such information.
The water quality reports analyzed should include one that includes
various parameters for obtaining a pollution index, and which presents the
information in non-technical form. In addition, data management through time
series is to be included.
9.    Quality control
Due to the fact that water quality monitoring entails the performance of
analyses in a considerable number of laboratories, by different analysts, a
basic analytical quality control program needs to be set up which is directed
toward obtaining reliable results.
The above program would include instructions for assessing the
reliability of the sample collection, field analysis, and preservation
techniques employed; the selection of analytical methodo, including a clear
description of those methods; an evaluation of solutions; the preparation of
control charts; an assessment of the precision of each laboratory; and a
review and comparison of systematic errors within and between the SEDUE
laboratories.
7.    Supplementary information
The placement of stations, determination of parameters, frequency of
sampling, and interpretation of the data obtained all require additional
information, in such areas as physiography, soil and water use, the location
of pollution sources, the identification of types of pollutants, demography,



- 113 -
annex_ 22
page 5 of 5
health infrastructure, the location of water vdevel,pment and wastewater
treatment works, as well at knowledge of any aquatic ecological systems of
exceptional or special interest. In designing the network, these needs will
have to be taken into account.
8.    Progress report and final report
In order to keep tabs on the study activities and assess their progress,
a timetable should be prepared so that it will be possible to detect any
necessary corrections and changes and to effect them in a timely manner.
The final report should contain accounts of the work carried out, the
methodologies used, the conclusions reached, and the pertinent
recommendations.
The report is to be illustrated with the necessary figures and diagrams-
-prepared to the appropriate scale--tables, charts, photographs, and graphs.
The diagrams submitted should include the originals and an exact copy.
The report submitted should include the original and 10 copies, in
addition to a summary booklet giving the background, the problems addressed,
and the objectives, results, conclusions, and recommendations, and it should
be illustrated with reduced drawings of any helpful figures and diagrams.
The manuals of operation and intersecretarial coordination which have
been prepared for all the various phases of operation of the National Water
Quality Monitoring Network should be submitted.
